Chapter of al-Ariyah: its definition and rulings. If he rented it out and it perished, the lender makes good its value, and he does not recourse against anyone, or the lessee, and the lessee recourses against his lessor if he did not know that it was a loan (ariyah) with him. And one may lend what whose usage differs or not if he did not specify a beneficiary. (If he rented it out and it perished, the lender makes good its value, and he does not recourse against anyone, or the lessee), in nasb as a conjunct to the accusative pronoun in 'yadmanuhu', and he recourses 'against his lessor if he did not know that it was a loan with him', if the lessee did not know that it was a loan with his lessor. He only recourses against him due to deception (ghurur), contrary to when he knows, for there is no deception from the lessor. (And one may lend what whose usage differs or not if he did not specify a beneficiary). It becomes binding, binding the binding of al-ariyah, which is contrary to its nature; because when the lease becomes binding, the loan becomes binding, due to the impossibility of taking it back. So the conclusion is that lending is inferior to leasing, and a thing does not include what is above it. [1] His saying: 'yadmanuhu al-mu'ir': meaning the lender makes liable the borrower or the lessee. The lender has a choice: if he wishes, he makes the borrower liable because he became a usurper by his transgression, for if the loan does not cover lease, it becomes usurpation; and if he wishes, he makes the lessee liable because he took possession without the owner's permission for himself. If he makes the borrower liable, he does not recourse with what he paid against anyone, i.e., against the lessee, and it is better to say thus, for there is no benefit in generalization; because it appears by the guarantee that the borrower rented out his own property, and he gives the rent in charity because he became like a usurper. And the usurper, if he rents out, owns the rent and gives it in charity because it accrued through a vile means, usage of another's property, and its way is charity, contrary to Abu Yusuf, and its mention has passed in the Book of Usurpation. And it is ... And if the lender makes the lessee liable, the lessee recourses against the lessor if he did not know that it was a loan in the hand of the borrower, to repel the harm of deception from the lessee, contrary to when the lessee knows that it was a loan in the hand of the lessor, for he does not recourse against him because no deception occurred from him. (2) His saying: 'and he recourses... etc.': meaning the lessee recourses against his lessor, who is the borrower, if the lessee did not know that it was a loan in the hand of the lessor, and he only recourses against him due to deception, contrary to when he knows. And one may lend what whose usage differs or not if he did not specify a beneficiary, and what does not differ if he specified, and likewise the lessor. (And one may lend what whose usage differs or not if he did not specify a beneficiary, and what does not differ if he specified): meaning if he lent a thing and did not specify who benefits from it, then the borrower may lend it, whether its usage differs like riding an animal, or does not differ like carrying a load on the animal. And if he specified who benefits from it, then if its usage does not differ, he may lend it; if it differs, he may not. (And likewise the lessor): i.e., if he leased a thing, then if he did not specify who benefits from it, the lessee may lend it, whether its usage differs or not; if he specified, he lends what does not differ, not what differs. And according to al-Shafi'i, it is not for the borrower to lend, because he does not recourse due to lack of deception. [11] His saying: i.e., if he lent...etc.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kitab al-Ariyah explains two interconnected rulings. The first ruling concerns a scenario where the borrower (musta'ir) rents out the borrowed item to a third party without the lender's permission, and the item is then destroyed. The lender (mu'ir) has the option to claim compensation for the value from either the borrower or the lessee (musta'jir). If the lender chooses to hold the borrower liable, the borrower becomes akin to a usurper (ghasib) and cannot recourse against anyone for the amount paid. Moreover, the rental income received by the borrower must be given in charity, as it is tainted by the unlawful use of another's property. This ruling on charity is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, with Abu Yusuf dissenting, as noted in the Book of Usurpation. If the lender chooses to hold the lessee liable, the lessee may, in turn, recourse against the borrower (who acted as lessor) for the compensation, provided the lessee was unaware that the item was borrowed. This recourse is based on the principle of deception (ghurur): the borrower deceived the lessee by presenting the item as his own to lease. If the lessee knew it was borrowed, there is no deception, and thus no recourse. The second ruling addresses the permissibility for the borrower to lend the borrowed item to someone else. The general rule is that if the lender did not specify a particular beneficiary (manfa'ah), the borrower may lend the item to others, regardless of whether the intended usage is similar or different. If the lender did specify a beneficiary, then the borrower may only lend the item if the usage does not differ from that specified; if the usage differs, he may not. For example, if the lender specifies that the animal is for the borrower's own riding, the borrower cannot lend it for carrying loads, but he could lend it for riding to another if the usage is identical. The same rule applies to a lessee (musta'jir) with respect to subleasing. The Shafi'i school opposes these rulings, holding that the borrower has no right to lend the borrowed item at all. The Hanafi position is justified by the general permission implied in the lender's lack of specification, and the borrower's freedom to benefit from the item within the scope of the loan.

Key Terms
عارية (ʿāriyah): A loan of a non-fungible item for use, without compensation, where the item is returned after use.
غرور (ghurūr): Deception or misrepresentation that induces a party into a contract; here it refers to the borrower deceiving the lessee by presenting the borrowed item as his own.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, if the borrower rents out the borrowed item without permission and it perishes, from whom can the lender seek compensation?
A) Only from the borrower
B) Only from the lessee
C) Either from the borrower or the lessee, at the lender's option
D) Neither, because the lender implicitly permitted the rental

Key Takeaway
The lender has the option to claim compensation from either the borrower or the lessee; the lessee's recourse against the borrower depends on his ignorance of the borrowed status.

Conditions for Re-Lending the Borrowed Item
Commentary on conditions for re-lending the borrowed item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi conditions under which a borrower may lend the borrowed item to another person, and distinguish the ruling when the lender specified a beneficiary versus when he did not.

Core Idea
Borrower may re-lend if lender did not specify a beneficiary, or if usage does not differ when specified.

Can you lend your friend's book to another friend? Islamic law gives a nuanced answer. It depends on whether the original lender specified who should use the item and whether the intended usage changes. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rules for re-lending and contrasts them with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the borrower (musta'ir) lending the borrowed item to a third party. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) under certain conditions.
This is a permissibility ruling, not one of obligation.
2
Distinguish the two cases: unspecified vs. specified beneficiary
If the lender (mu'ir) did not specify a particular beneficiary (manfa'ah), the borrower may lend the item to anyone, regardless of whether the usage differs or not. If the lender did specify a beneficiary, then the borrower may only lend the item if the intended usage is the same as that specified.
The key variable is the lender's specification.
3
Apply the rule using examples
Example: Lender says 'Ride my horse' (no beneficiary specified). Borrower can lend it to another for riding or even for carrying loads. If lender says 'Ride my horse yourself', borrower cannot lend it for carrying loads (different usage), but may lend it for riding by another (same usage).
Usage difference matters only when a beneficiary is specified.
4
Extend the rule to the lessee (musta'jir)
The same rule applies to a lessee who has leased an item: if the lessor did not specify a user, the lessee may sublease; if a user was specified, subleasing is only allowed if the usage does not differ.
This symmetry shows consistency in Hanafi contract law.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i holds that the borrower can never lend the borrowed item to another, as the borrower's possession is a trust (amanah) that does not extend to sub-lending. The Hanafi view is based on general permission implied by the lender's silence.
The dispute is over the nature of the borrower's right: restricted trust vs. full use unless limited.

The first ruling concerns a scenario where the borrower (musta'ir) rents out the borrowed item to a third party without the lender's permission, and the item is then destroyed. This recourse is based on the principle of deception (ghurur): the borrower deceived the lessee by presenting the item as his own to lease. منتفع (muntafiʿ): Beneficiary; the person who is allowed to use the borrowed or leased item.

Lender's Liability for Diminution: Timed vs Untimed Loans
Ruling on lender's liability in 'ariyah based on timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lender's liability for diminution in 'ariyah and explain why it differs between timed and untimed loans based on the principle of ghurur.

Core Idea
In an untimed loan, the borrower bears the risk; in a timed loan, the lender's early recovery triggers liability for deception.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi distinction in the law of 'ariyah (non-binding loan). The ruling hinges on whether the loan was fixed with a time limit or not. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how the Hanafi school balances the lender's right to revoke with the borrower's reliance interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the ruling: untimed loan (muṭlaq) vs timed loan (muwaqqat). Note the different outcomes: no liability vs liability for diminution.
The ruling is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Understand the reason for the difference
The key concept is ghurur (deception). In a timed loan, the lender's promise creates reasonable reliance; early recovery makes him a deceiver. In an untimed loan, the borrower knows the loan can be revoked at any time, so he deceives himself.
The author explicitly says 'إنما يضمن للغرور' – liability is because of deception.
3
Examine the opposing views
Note Imam Malik's view (full liability, structures remain) and Zufar's view (no liability in either). Understand the Hanafi response to each.
These differences are part of the broader ikhtilaf within and outside the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the ruling to a numerical example
The passage gives an example: if the standing value is 10 dinars and after removal it decreases by 2 dinars, the lender must pay 8 dinars. Practice this calculation.
This is the amount of diminution, not the full value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يضمن إن أطلق، وضمن ما نقص بالقلع إن وقت. ولا يضمن إن أطلق: أي لا يضمن المعير ما نقص من البناء والغرس بالقلع، إن كانت الإعارة مطلقة: أي غير مؤقتة. وضمن ما نقص بالقلع إن وقت: أي وقت الإعارة، ورجع عنها قبل ذلك الوقت. وقال مالك: يلزمه ضمان قيمتها، ويتركان في أرضه؛ لأنه صار مغروراً من جهته، فيلزمه الضمان كما إذا وقت للعارية وقتاً، فرجع قبل الوقت. قلنا: العارية غير لازمة، فيكون له الرجوع في كل وقت، فلم يكن غاراً له بالإطلاق، وإنما هو اغتر بنفسه، بخلاف ما إذا كانت مؤقتة فرجع قبل الوقت؛ لأنه يصير غاراً له بذلك، حيث نص على تركها في يده إلى الوقت المذكور. وهذا لأن ظاهر حال المسلم أن يفي بالوعد فيكون مغرورا من جهته، وكان له أن يرجع عليه دفعاً للضرر عن نفسه بسببه. كذا في التبيين وغيره. وضمن ما نقص بالقلع؛ أي وضمن المعير للمستعير ما نقص من البناء والغرس بالقلع، بأن يقوم قائماً غير مقلوع يعني إذا كانت قيمة البناء إلى الوقت المضروب عشرة دنانير مثلا، وإذا قلع في الحال يكون قيمة النقص دينارين، يرجع المستعير على المعير بثمانية دينار، وهذا ما ذكره القدوري في مختصره، واختاره صاحب الكنز والهداية وغيره. وكره الرجوع قبله، ولو أعار للزرع لا يؤخذ حتى يحصد وقت أو لا وإنما يضمن للغرور، وفي صورة الإطلاق ما غره، بل اغتر المستعير، واعتمد على الإطلاق، وكره الرجوع قبله: أي قبل الوقت؛ لأن فيه خلف الوعد. وقال زفر: لا يضمن؛ لأن التوقيت والإطلاق في العارية سواء لبطلان التأجيل في العواري. ولنا: ما ذكره الشارح بقوله: وإنما يضمن للغرور، وحاصله: أن المعير بالتوقيت غار للمستعير؛ لأنه نص على ترك الأرض في يده، وإقرار بنائه أو غرسه في المدة التي سماها. وللمغرور أن يدفع الضرر عن نفسه بالرجوع على الغار، بخلاف صورة الإطلاق؛ لأن المعير ما غره بل المستعير قد اغتر؛ لاعتماده على الإطلاق، فالضرر لزمه من عند نفسه، لا من عند المعير.
He is not liable if [the loan] is untimed, and he is liable for the diminution [caused] by removal if [the loan] is timed. 'He is not liable if untimed' means the lender is not liable for the diminution of the building and planting due to removal, if the loan was untimed, i.e., not fixed with a time. 'And he is liable for the diminution by removal if timed' means if the loan was timed, and he recovers it before that time. Malik said: He is liable for its full value, and [the structures] are left in his land because he became deceived by him, so liability is incumbent on him just as if he fixed a time for the loan and then recovered before the time. We said: The loan is non-binding, so he has the right to recover at any time, thus he was not a deceiver of him in the untimed case; rather, the borrower deceived himself. This differs from the timed case where he recovers before the time, because he becomes a deceiver by that, as he specified leaving it in his hand until that time. This is because the apparent state of a Muslim is to fulfill promises, so he becomes deceived by him, and he has the right to sue him to prevent harm to himself due to that. As in al-Tabyeen and others. 'And he is liable for the diminution by removal' means the lender is liable to the borrower for what diminished of the building and planting due to removal, by valuing it standing unremoved. For example, if the value of the building for the stipulated time is ten dinars, and when removed immediately, the value of the diminution is two dinars, the borrower sues the lender for eight dinars. This is what al-Quduri mentioned in his Mukhtasar, and it was chosen by the author of al-Kanz and al-Hidayah and others. And it is disliked to recover before it. 'And if he lends for cultivation, he is not taken until harvest, whether timed or not. He is only liable for the deception.' In the untimed case, he did not deceive him; rather the borrower deceived himself, relying on the untimed nature, and it is disliked to recover before it, i.e., before the time, because it is breach of promise. Zufar said: He is not liable because timing and untiming are equal in loan, due to the invalidity of stipulating a term in loans. Our argument is what the commentator mentioned: 'He is only liable for the deception,' the gist of which is that the lender by timing deceives the borrower because he specified leaving the land in his hand and affirming his building or planting for the duration he named. The deceived person can avoid harm by suing the deceiver, unlike the untimed case, because the lender did not deceive him; rather the borrower deceived himself by relying on the untimed nature, so the harm came from himself, not from the lender.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the lender's liability for the diminution of structures (buildings or trees) placed by the borrower on loaned land. The ruling distinguishes between an untimed loan ('ariyah mutlaqa) and a timed loan ('ariyah muwaqqata). In an untimed loan, the lender may reclaim the land at any time and is not liable for any loss the borrower incurs from removing his structures; the borrower acted at his own risk. In a timed loan, however, if the lender recovers the land before the agreed term, he must compensate the borrower for the decrease in value caused by the removal (e.g., by uprooting trees or dismantling buildings). The compensation is the difference between the value of the structures standing (at the time of removal) and their value after removal. The passage then addresses two opposing views: Imam Malik holds that the lender must pay the full value of the structures and let them remain, because the lender is considered to have deceived the borrower by making him invest. The Hanafi response is that an untimed loan is not binding, so there is no deception; the borrower assumed the risk. In a timed loan, the lender's promise creates reasonable reliance, hence deception and liability. Later, Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) argues that there is no liability in either case because a loan contract cannot include a valid stipulation of term. The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) rejects this, insisting that timing does create liability due to deception (ghurur). The relied-upon fatwa is the majority view. The passage also notes that it is disliked (makruh tanzihan) for the lender to recover before the agreed time, as it involves breaking a promise.

Key Terms
عارية ('ariyah): A contract where the owner allows another to use a non-consumable item for free, with the item being returned after use.
ضمن (daman): Liability to pay compensation for loss or diminution in value.
غرور (ghurur): Deception that induces a person to act to his detriment based on a false impression created by another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person lends his land to another for planting trees without specifying any time limit, and then recovers the land after one year, is he liable to compensate the borrower for the value of the trees removed?
A) No, because it was an untimed loan.
B) Yes, full value of the trees.
C) Yes, only the diminution caused by removal.
D) Yes, but only if the borrower demands it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between timed and untimed 'ariyah is grounded in the principle of deception (ghurur), which triggers liability only when the lender's promise creates reasonable reliance.

Land Lent for Cultivation: Right to Stay Until Harvest (Istihsan)
Ruling with proof by istihsan on borrower's right to stay until harvest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on borrowing land for cultivation and apply the istihsan reasoning that allows the borrower to remain until harvest with payment of market rent.

Core Idea
The borrower may stay until harvest but must pay rent—istihsan prevents harm while ensuring fairness.

When land is lent for cultivation, a conflict arises if the lender demands return before the crop is harvested. The Hanafi school resolves this by istihsan: the borrower cannot be evicted until harvest, because eviction would destroy the crop and cause unjust harm. However, to prevent the lender's benefit from being lost, the borrower must pay the market rental value for the extra period. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Conflict
The act is lending land specifically for cultivation. The conflict is the lender's demand for return before harvest.
The loan may be timed or untimed.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The borrower cannot be evicted until harvest, by istihsan. The lender must allow the crop to mature.
This applies regardless of whether a time was fixed.
3
Locate the Proof (Dalil of Istihsan)
The proof is the principle that harming a believer is forbidden (لأن الإضرار بالمؤمن حرام). Strict analogy would allow eviction, but istihsan prevents harm.
Thubut and dalala are zanni; istihsan overrides qiyas.
4
Identify the Consequence: Rent Payment
To balance interests, the borrower must pay the lender the market rental value (ujrat al-mithl) for the period he remains after the loan's expiration or demand.
The loan effectively becomes a hire contract for that period.
5
Consider Nuances (Structures and Trees)
If the borrower has planted trees or built structures, he may remove them or claim compensation. If removal harms the land, the lender decides.
This shows the Hanafi attention to preventing harm to both parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أعار للزرع لا يؤخذ حتى يحصد وقت أو لا. وإنما يضمن للغرور، وفي صورة الإطلاق ما غره، بل اغتر المستعير، واعتمد على الإطلاق، وكره الرجوع قبله: أي قبل الوقت؛ لأن فيه خلف الوعد. ( ولو أعارَ للزرع لا يُؤخَدُ) حتى يُحْصَدَ وقت أو لا ) وقال في الهداية: وذكر الحاكم الشهيد له: إنه يضمن رب الأرض للمستعير قيمة غرسه وبنائه، ويكون له إلا أن يشاءَ المستعير أن يرفعهما، ولا يضمنه قيمتهما، فيكون له ذلك؛ لأنه ملكه. انتهى. وحاصله: أن المستعير في هذه الصورة بالخيار إن شاء ضمن صاحب الأرض قيمة غرسه وبنائه قائماً مبيناً، وترك ذلك عليه، ويملكها بأداء الضمان، وإن شاء أخذ غرسه وبناءه، ولا شيء على صاحب الأرض، وقيل: أن ضر يخير المعير بين ضمان ما نقص، وضمان القيمة. وقال في الهداية: قالوا: إذا كان في القلع ضر بالأرض فالخيار إلى رب الأرض؛ لأنه صاحب الأصل والمستعير صاحب تبع، والترجيح للأصل. انتهى. قال الشارح: لا يؤخذ أي المستعار استحساناً؛ لأن الإضرار بالمؤمن حرام، فيترك في يده بطريق الإجارة بأجر المثل، حتى يحصد الزرع كيلا تفوت منفعة أرضه مجاناً.
And if he lends for cultivation, he is not taken until harvest, whether timed or not. And he is only liable for the deception. In the untimed case he did not deceive him; rather the borrower deceived himself and relied on the untimed nature, and it is disliked to recover before it, i.e., before the time, because it is breach of promise. And it is said in al-Hidayah: Al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned from him: The owner of the land guarantees to the borrower the value of his planting and building, and it becomes his [the borrower's] unless the borrower wishes to remove them, and he does not guarantee their value, so he has that right because he owns them. End. Its summary: The borrower in this situation has a choice: if he wishes, he makes the landowner liable for the value of his planting and building while standing and described, and leaves them, and he owns them by paying the guarantee; and if he wishes, he takes his planting and building, and nothing is owed by the landowner. And it was said: If there is harm [from removal], the lender has the option between guaranteeing the diminution and guaranteeing the value. And it is said in al-Hidayah: They said: If there is harm to the land from removal, then the option is with the owner of the land, because he is the owner of the original [land], and the borrower is subordinate, and preference is given to the original. End. The commentator said: 'He is not taken' means the borrowed [land] by istihsan, because harming a believer is forbidden, so it is left in his hand by way of hire with an equivalent rent, until the crop is harvested, so that the benefit of his land is not lost for free.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: land lent for cultivation (to grow crops). The core ruling is that the borrower cannot be evicted from the land until he harvests the crop, regardless of whether the loan was timed or untimed (istihsan). The reason is that causing harm to a believer is forbidden; evicting the borrower before harvest would destroy the crop. To balance interests, the borrower may remain until harvest, but he must pay rent for the land (ujrat al-mithl) for the extra period after the loan's term (or if untimed, after the lender demands it). This effectively converts the loan into a hire contract for the remainder of the growing period. The passage then discusses nuances: if the borrower has planted trees or built structures, he has the option to either remove them or leave them and take compensation (value of structures as standing). However, if removal would harm the land (e.g., damaging soil), the landowner has the final choice. This discussion ties back to the earlier liability rules. The relied-upon position is that the borrower may stay until harvest but must pay rent to avoid loss to the lender.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference used to reach a more equitable ruling than strict analogy; here used to allow the borrower to stay until harvest.
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): The market rental value for similar land; the borrower must pay this for the period he remains on the land after the lender's demand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only after the wheat is harvested.

Key Takeaway
By istihsan, a borrower of land for cultivation may remain until harvest but must pay the market rental value.

Who Bears the Cost of Returning Items? Borrowed, Rented, and Usurped
States who bears return costs for borrowed, rented, usurped items with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for bearing return costs for borrowed, rented, and usurped items and explain the reasoning based on the benefit of possession.

Core Idea
The cost of returning an item falls on the person obligated to physically return it.

In Hanafi fiqh, when an item is in another's possession—whether through borrowing, renting, or usurpation—the question arises: who pays for its return to the owner? This lesson clarifies the rule, which hinges on who is obligated to physically return the item and for whose benefit the possession was taken. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion, especially in rental contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possession types
List the three scenarios: borrowed (ariyah), rented (ijarah), and usurped (ghasb). For each, note the person in possession: borrower (musta'ir), lessee (musta'jir with kasra), and usurper (ghasib).
2
State the general rule
The expense of returning the item is borne by the person who is legally obligated to physically return it. This obligation in turn depends on whose benefit the possession serves.
3
Apply to borrower and usurper
The borrower took possession for his own benefit, so returning is obligatory upon him; similarly, the usurper wrongfully took possession, so return is obligatory on him. Thus, they bear the cost.
4
Apply to the lessee
The lessee's obligation is only to enable possession (tamkin wa takhliyah) by vacating; the physical return is not obligatory on him. The benefit of possession during the lease belongs to the lessor (who receives rent), so the lessor must bear the cost of return.
The term musta'jir is ambiguous: with fatha on jim it means lessor, with kasra it means lessee.
5
Understand the talil (reasoning)
The ruling is based on a simple principle: the one who benefits from possession must bear the cost of return. For borrower and usurper, the benefit is theirs; for lessee, the benefit belongs to the lessor (the rent is the lessor's compensation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجرة رد المستعار والمستأجر والمغصوب على المستعير والمؤجر والغاصب) ؛ لأن " الرَّدَّ واجب على المستعير والغاصب عند طلب المالك، وأماً على المستأجر التمكينُ والتَّخلية دون الرّدّ ، فإنَّ منفعة القبض للمؤجر، فتكونُ مؤنة الرد عليه لا على المستأجر.  ٢ ] قوله : لأنّ...الخ ; يعني لأن رد المستعار واجب على المستعير; لأنه قبض العارية لمنفعة نفسه، فتكون أجرة الردّ عليه ، وردُّ المغصوب إلى مالكه واجب على الغاصب، فيكون عليه مؤنة ردّه. وأما أجرة رد المستأجر - بفتح الجيم - علي المؤجر ، فللأنَّ المستأجر - بكسر الجيم - قبضه لمنفعة المؤجر ; لأنَّ الأجر سلم له، فلا يكون رده واجباً عليه، بل على المؤجر، فتكون مؤنة رده عليه.
And the expense of returning the borrowed, rented, and usurped [item] is upon the borrower, the lessor, and the usurper respectively; because returning is obligatory upon the borrower and the usurper when the owner demands it. For the lessee, it is enabling and vacating, not returning, because the benefit of possession is for the lessor, so the cost of returning falls on him, not on the lessee. [PAGE_447] 2] His statement: Because...; meaning because returning the borrowed item is obligatory upon the borrower, since he took possession for his own benefit, so the cost of returning is upon him. Returning the usurped item to its owner is obligatory upon the usurper, so he bears the cost of its return. As for the expense of returning the rented item - with fatha on the jim [musta'jir = lessor] - it is upon the lessor, because the lessee - with kasra on the jim [musta'jir = lessee] - took possession for the benefit of the lessor, because the rent is secure for him, so returning is not obligatory upon him, but rather upon the lessor, so he bears the cost of its return.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with evident reasoning. It clarifies who bears the cost of returning an item to its owner in three types of possession: borrowing ('ariyah), renting (ijarah), and usurpation (ghasb). The rule is: the person who is obligated to physically return the item bears the cost. For a borrower (musta'ir), returning is obligatory because he took possession for his own benefit, so he pays for transport. For a usurper (ghasib), returning is obligatory due to wrongdoing, so he pays. For a lessee (musta'jir, breaker of the jim), however, his obligation is merely to enable the lessor to take possession (tamkin wa takhliyah) by vacating; the actual physical return is the lessor's responsibility because the benefit of possession (manfa'at al-qabd) belongs to the lessor (the rent is secure). Therefore, the cost of returning a rented item (e.g., delivering it back) falls on the lessor (mu'jir), not the lessee. The commentary confirms the reasoning: the borrower took possession for himself, the usurper took wrongfully, so they pay; the lessee took possession for the lessor's benefit (since he paid rent), so the lessor pays.

Key Terms
المستأجر (al-musta’jir): The lessor (with fatha on the jim) - the person who rents out an item.
المستعير (al-musta'ir): The borrower - the person who borrows an item for free.
الغاصب (al-ghasib): The usurper - one who takes another's property without permission.
المؤجر (al-mu'jir): The lessor - one who rents out property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The owner.
B) The borrower.
C) The lessor.
D) Both owner and borrower.

Key Takeaway
The cost of returning an item is borne by the party obligated to physically return it, which depends on whose benefit the possession serves.

Cost of Returning Borrowed, Rented, and Usurped Items
Ruling on who bears the return cost for borrowed, rented, and usurped items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who bears the return cost for borrowed, rented, and usurped items and explain the legal principle that determines the obligation.

Core Idea
The burden of return follows the benefit: the party who benefits bears the cost.

When an item has been possessed by another person—whether as a loan, rental, or usurpation—the question arises: who must pay for its return? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya provides the Hanafi ruling based on a simple but powerful principle: the one who benefits from the possession shoulders the expense of returning it. Understanding this rule clarifies many daily transactions and liability cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of possession
The passage covers borrowed (mustaʿār), rented (mustaʾjar), and usurped (maghsūb) items. Note the different Arabic terms.
Each category has a distinct ruling.
2
For each category, identify who bears the return cost
Cost is on borrower, lessor, and usurper respectively. Memorize this tripartite rule.
The lessor bears cost, not the lessee.
3
Identify the underlying rationale (taʿlīl)
The benefit principle: borrower benefits himself, usurper benefits himself, lessee benefits the lessor (since rent is paid to lessor). Therefore the burden follows benefit.
This is a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī).
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
For example, borrowing a book: borrower benefits so he pays return postage. Renting an apartment: lessor benefits from rent so he pays major return costs; lessee only enables return.
Contracts may modify the default ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجرة رد المستعار والمستأجر والمغصوب على المستعير والمؤجر والغاصب.
[٢] قوله : لأنّ...الخ ; يعني لأن رد المستعار واجب على المستعير؛ لأنه قبض العارية لمنفعة نفسه، فتكون أجرة الردّ عليه، وردُّ المغصوب إلى مالكه واجب على الغاصب، فيكون عليه مؤنة ردّه. وأما أجرة رد المستأجر - بفتح الجيم - علي المؤجر ، فللأنَّ المستأجر - بكسر الجيم - قبضه لمنفعة المؤجر ; لأنَّ الأجر سلم له، فلا يكون رده واجباً عليه، بل على المؤجر، فتكون مؤنة رده عليه.
And the cost of returning the borrowed item, the rented item, and the usurped item is upon the borrower, the lessor, and the usurper respectively.
[2] His statement: 'because...' etc.; meaning because returning the borrowed item is obligatory upon the borrower, as he took possession of the loan for his own benefit, so the cost of returning it is upon him. Returning the usurped item to its owner is obligatory upon the usurper, so the expense of returning it is upon him. As for the cost of returning the rented item (with fat-ha on the jim) is upon the lessor, because the lessee (with kasr on the jim) took possession for the benefit of the lessor; because the rent is delivered to him, so returning it is not obligatory upon him, but upon the lessor, so the expense of returning it is upon him.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on who bears the expense of returning items that were in one's possession. The cost of returning a borrowed item (عارية) is on the borrower (مستعير) because he took it for his own benefit. The cost of returning a usurped item (مغصوب) is on the usurper (غاصب). However, for a rented item (مستأجر), the cost of return is on the lessor (مؤجر) because the lessee (مستأجر) took possession for the benefit of the lessor (since the rent was paid). The lessee's obligation is only to enable the lessor to take possession (تمكين وتخلية), not to physically return.

Key Terms
مستعار (mustaʿār): The item given as a loan (ʿāriyah) for use without rent.
مستأجر (mustaʾjar (with fatha) / mustaʾjir (with kasra)): The rented item (mustaʾjar) and the lessee (mustaʾjir). Clarifies that the return cost is on the lessor (muʾjir).
مغصوب (maghsūb): An item taken forcibly or wrongfully (ghasb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The cost of returning a possessed item falls on the party who benefits from the possession: the borrower, the usurper, and the lessor in hire contracts.

The Placement and Definition of Ijarah
Introduction explaining placement and definition of Ijarah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three reasons for placing Ijarah after Hibah and define Ijarah linguistically and legally including its rukn.

Core Idea
Ijarah is a sale of usufruct; its fiqh placement reflects the conceptual priority of assets over benefits.

This lesson opens the Book of Ijarah by explaining why it follows Hibah (gift) in classical Hanafi texts. The author provides three conceptual reasons based on the nature of the subject matter and compensation. Then he moves to the linguistic and legal definitions of Ijarah, identifies its essential pillar (rukn), and introduces its two fundamental types: usufruct of assets and human labor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ordering rationale
List the three reasons the author gives for placing Ijarah after Hibah: (1) Hibah transfers the specific thing ('ayn), Ijarah transfers usufruct (manfa'ah); the specific thing is stronger. (2) Specific things exist before usufructs. (3) Hibah is originally without compensation, Ijarah has compensation; absence precedes existence. Also note the connection with Sadaqah as both are binding contracts.
The author uses a logical progression from stronger to weaker and from absence to existence.
2
Distinguish linguistic and legal definitions
Linguistically, Ijarah is related to reward (ujrah). Legally, it is a sale of usufruct for a known compensation, as stated by the author: 'It is a sale...' The definition emphasizes that Ijarah is a type of sale (bay'), transferring ownership of a benefit.
The legal definition is taken from the matn (primary text) being commented upon.
3
Identify the rukn (pillar)
The rukn of Ijarah is the offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) using specific terms. This is common to all contracts of exchange.
Details of the terms are found in more extensive works (al-Mabsut).
4
Recognize the two types
Ijarah is divided into two categories: (1) over the usufruct of specific assets (e.g., renting a house, land, animals), and (2) over human labor (e.g., hiring a tailor or launderer). Both are valid forms of Ijarah.
This classification affects how conditions and rulings are applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة ) [١) قوله : كتاب الإجارة؛ لَمَّا كانت الهبةُ تشارك الإجارة في معنى التمليك، وكانت الهبة تمليك عين، والإجارة تمليك منفعة أَخَّرَ الإجارة عن الهبة لكون العين أقوى، وأيضاً الأعيان مقدَّمة وجوداً على المنافع، وأيضاً الأصل في الهبة عدم العوض، وفي الإجارة العوض، والعدم مقدم على الوجود، ثمّ لعقد الإجارة مناسبة خاصة ببيان الصدقة من حيث إنهما يقعان لازمين، فلذلك أورد «كتاب الإجارة» متصلاً بذكرِ الصدقة. والإجارة لغة اسم للأجرة، وما هو يستحق على عمل الخير، ألا ترى أنه يقال : أعظم الله أجرك. وشرعاً ما ذكره المصنف ه بقوله : وهي بيع يقع... الخ. وركنها : هو الإيجاب والقبول بالألفاظ الموضوعة في عقد الإجارة، وتفصيلهما مذكور في المبسوطات. وهي على نوعين : ١. نوع يرد على منافع الأعيان؛ كاستئجار الدار والأراضي والدواب ونحوها. ونوع يردّ على العمل : كاستئجار المحترفين للأعمال؛ كالقصارة والخياطة ونحوهما. وحكمها : وقوع الملكِ في البدلين ساعة فساعة إلا بشرط تعجيل الإجارة، وكيفية انعقادها أنها تنعقد فيما بين المتعاقدين للحال، وتنعقد ساعة فساعة في حق الحكم، وهو الملك على حسب حدوث المنفعة. وصفتها : أنها عقد لازم إن كانت صحيحة عرية عن خيار الشرط والعيب والرؤية، وما صح أن يكون ثمناً في البيع : كالنقود والمكيل والموزون صح أن يكون أجرةً في الإجارة، وما لا يصح ثمناً صلح أجرةً أيضاً : كالأعيان مثل العبيد والثياب. صرح به في «الكافي»، وغيره. وأما شرائطها فهي على أنواع شرائط الانعقاد، وشرائط النفاذ، وشرائط الصحة، وشرائط اللزوم.
Book of Ijarah. [1] His statement: 'Book of Ijarah'. Since Hibah (gift) and Ijarah share the meaning of transfer of ownership, and Hibah is transfer of a specific thing ('ayn) while Ijarah is transfer of usufruct (manfa'ah), he placed Ijarah after Hibah because the specific thing is stronger. Also, specific things exist before usufructs. Also, the original in Hibah is without compensation, while in Ijarah there is compensation, and absence precedes existence. Moreover, the contract of Ijarah has a special connection to the mention of Sadaqah in that both become binding. Therefore, he brought the 'Book of Ijarah' connected to the mention of Sadaqah. Ijarah linguistically is a noun for the wage (ujrah) and what is deserved for a good deed. Do you not see that it is said: 'May Allah reward you greatly'? Legally, it is what the author mentioned: 'It is a sale...' etc. Its pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance with the words used in the contract of Ijarah; their detail is mentioned in the extensive works. It is of two types: 1. A type that applies to the usufruct of specific things, like renting a house, land, animals, etc. And a type that applies to work, like hiring professionals for work, such as laundering, tailoring, etc. Its ruling is the ownership of the two compensations moment by moment, unless there is a condition of immediate payment of the wage. Its manner of conclusion is that it is concluded between the contracting parties for the present, and it is concluded moment by moment in terms of the ruling, which is ownership based on the occurrence of the benefit. Its description: It is a binding contract (lazim) if it is valid and free from the options of stipulation, defect, and sight. Whatever is valid as a price in sale, such as currency, measured items, and weighed items, is valid as a wage in Ijarah. Whatever is not valid as a price is also valid as a wage, such as specific things like slaves and clothes. This is explicitly stated in al-Kafi and others. As for its conditions, they are of types: conditions of inception, conditions of effectiveness, conditions of validity, and conditions of bindingness.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the Book of Ijarah in the commentary. The author first explains why Ijarah is placed after Hibah (gift) and before Sadaqah (charity). He gives three reasons: (1) Ijarah involves usufruct while Hibah involves the specific thing itself; the specific thing is stronger and comes first. (2) Specific things exist prior to usufructs. (3) Hibah is originally without compensation, while Ijarah involves compensation; absence (of compensation) is given precedence over existence. Also, both Ijarah and Sadaqah are binding contracts, so they are placed together. He then provides the linguistic and legal definition of Ijarah. Linguistically, it is related to reward (ujrah); legally, it is a sale of usufruct. The pillar (rukn) of the contract is offer and acceptance with specific words. Ijarah is of two types: (1) over the usufruct of tangible assets (like houses, land, animals), and (2) over work (like hiring a tailor). The ruling: ownership of the two compensations (the benefit and the wage) occurs moment by moment as the benefit is realized. The contract is binding if valid and free from options of stipulation, defect, or sight. Any item that can be a price in sale (cash, measured or weighed goods) can be a wage in Ijarah; even items not valid as a price (like slaves or clothes) can be a wage. Finally, the conditions for Ijarah are categorized into conditions for inception (shurut al-in'iqad), effectiveness (nifadh), validity (sihhah), and bindingness (luzum).

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): Legally, a sale of usufruct for a known compensation.
منفعة (manfa'ah): The benefit or utility derived from an object or service; the subject matter of ijarah.
عقد لازم (aqd lazim): A binding contract that cannot be revoked unilaterally except by mutual consent or legal cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The wage and the benefit
B) Offer and acceptance
C) The specific asset
D) The presence of compensation

Key Takeaway
Ijarah is a sale of usufruct, placed after Hibah due to the conceptual priority of assets over usufructs and absence over presence of compensation.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease

The Ruling and Conditions of Ijarah
Ruling on ownership, wage conditions, and binding nature of Ijarah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how ownership occurs in Ijarah, identify valid wages, and list the four categories of conditions.

Core Idea
In Ijarah, ownership of the wage and benefit is incremental; the contract is binding and requires clear conditions.

Having defined Ijarah and its types, the passage now details its ruling: ownership of the two compensations occurs moment by moment as the benefit is realized. The contract is binding if valid, free from certain stipulations. We also learn what items can serve as a wage—anything that can be a price in a sale and even items that cannot. Finally, the conditions of Ijarah are categorized into four types: inception, effectiveness, validity, and bindingness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ruling on ownership
The ruling states that ownership of the wage (ujrah) and the benefit (manfa'ah) occurs moment by moment (saa'atan fa saa'atan) as the benefit is delivered. This means if the contract ends prematurely, the wage is owed only for the period used, unless there is a condition for immediate payment.
This incremental ownership is a key feature distinguishing Ijarah from sale of a tangible item.
2
Identify valid wages
Any item that can be a price in a sale (e.g., currency, measured/weighed goods) is valid as a wage. Even items that cannot be a price (e.g., specific objects like slaves, clothes) can be a wage. The wage must be known and deliverable.
The passage references al-Kafi for this rule.
3
Understand the binding nature
A valid Ijarah is a binding contract (lazim) if it is free from options of stipulation, defect, or sight. This means neither party can unilaterally cancel without cause.
If any of these options exist, the contract may be non-binding for the option holder.
4
List the four categories of conditions
Conditions of Ijarah are divided into four types: conditions of inception (shurut al-in'iqad), conditions of effectiveness (shurut al-nifadh), conditions of validity (shurut al-sihhah), and conditions of bindingness (shurut al-luzum). Each category addresses different aspects of the contract's formation and enforceability.
Detailed lists are beyond this introductory passage.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease

Definition, Types, and Ruling of Ijarah
Defines ijarah, explains its place in fiqh, classifies types, and outlines the legal description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ijarah, differentiate between its two types (benefits of things and benefits of labor), and explain why ijarah is considered a sale of benefits.

Core Idea
Ijarah is a sale of benefits – a transfer of usufruct for a fee, binding once valid and free from options.

This passage opens the chapter on ijarah (hiring/leasing) by placing it in relation to gift and charity, then gives both linguistic and legal definitions. The author classifies ijarah into two types and describes its legal effect as a binding, moment-by-moment transfer of ownership. Understanding this foundational definition is essential before delving into the detailed conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the placement of the ijarah chapter
Notice that the author explains why ijarah comes after hibah (gift) and before sadaqah (charity). This shows the systematic reasoning in the arrangement of fiqh chapters.
Ijarah shares with hibah the concept of ownership transfer, but hibah transfers a physical object, ijarah transfers a benefit.
2
Extract the legal definition
The author quotes the matn: 'It is a sale that occurs...' – implying ijarah is a sale of benefits. The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance using specific words.
Hanafi fiqh treats ijarah as a type of sale, which has implications for conditions and rulings.
3
Distinguish the two types of ijarah
Type 1: hiring the benefits of tangible assets (houses, land, animals). Type 2: hiring labor (laundering, tailoring). This distinction affects how the subject matter is specified.
Both types fall under the same rules, but the object (manfa'ah) differs: either derived from an 'ayn or from a person's work.
4
Understand the ruling (hukm) and description (sifah)
Ownership of fee and benefit transfers moment by moment unless immediate payment is stipulated. The contract is binding (lazim) if valid and free from options of condition, defect, or inspection.
Binding nature means neither party can unilaterally rescind after the contract is concluded.
5
Note what can serve as a fee (ajr)
Anything valid as a price in a sale (currency, measured/weighed items) is valid as a fee. Items like slaves or garments are not generally used as prices and thus invalid as fees.
This reflects the principle that ijarah is a sale of benefits, so the fee follows the rules of price in sale.

لَمَّا كانت الهبةُ تشارك الإجارة في معنى التمليك، وكانت الهبة تمليك عين، والإجارة تمليك منفعة أَخَّرَ الإجارة عن الهبة لكون العين أقوى، وأيضاً الأعيان مقدَّمة وجوداً على المنافع، وأيضاً الأصل في الهبة عدم العوض، وفي الإجارة العوض، والعدم مقدم على الوجود، ثمّ لعقد الإجارة مناسبة خاصة ببيان الصدقة من حيث إنهما يقعان لازمين، فلذلك أورد «كتاب الإجارة» متصلاً بذكرِ الصدقة.

وصفتها : أنها عقد لازم إن كانت صحيحة عرية عن خيار الشرط والعيب والرؤية، وما صح أن يكون ثمناً في البيع : كالنقود والمكيل والموزون صح أن يكون أجرةً في الإجارة، وما لا يصح ثمناً صلح أجرةً أيضاً : كالأعيان مثل العبيد والثياب. صرح به في «الكافي»، وغيره.

أما شرائط الانعقاد :
١. فمنها : العقل حتى لا تنعقد من المجنون والصبي الذي لا يعقل، والبلوغ ليس من شرائطها حتى أنّ الصبي العاقل لو أجر ماله أو نفسه فإن كان مأذوناً تنفذ، وإلا تقف على إجازة الولي.

٢. ومنها : الملك والولاية حتى لا تنفذ إجارة الفضولي ؛ لعدم الملك والولاية، لكنه ينعقد موقوفاً على إجازة المالك عندنا.

٢. ومنها : أن يكون المعقود عليه وهو المنافع معلوماً علماً يمنع المنازعة، فإن كان مجهولاً جهالة تفضي إلى المنازعة يمنع صحة العقد، وإلا فلا.
٣. ومنها : محل بيان المنفعة، حتى لو قال : أجرتُك إحدى هاتين الدارين لا تصح.
٤. ومنها : بيانُ المدَّة في الدور المنازل ونحوها وفي استئجار الظئر.
Book of Ijarah (Hiring). Since gift shares with ijarah in the meaning of ownership, and gift is ownership of a thing, while ijarah is ownership of benefit, he delayed ijarah after gift because a thing is stronger, and also things are prior in existence to benefits, and also the basis in gift is absence of consideration, while in ijarah is consideration, and absence is prior to existence. Then the contract of ijarah has a specific connection to the exposition of sadaqah because both are binding contracts, therefore he introduced the 'Book of Ijarah' connected to the mention of sadaqah. Ijarah linguistically is a name for the fee, and for what is due for good work; do you not see that it is said: 'May Allah increase your reward (ajr).' Legally, it is what the author mentioned: 'It is a sale that occurs...' etc. Its pillar is the offer and acceptance with the words established in the contract of ijarah, and their detail is mentioned in the comprehensive works. It is of two types: 1) A type that pertains to benefits of things, like hiring a house, land, animals, etc. And a type that pertains to work, like hiring professionals for works such as laundering, tailoring, etc. Its ruling: Ownership of both countervalues occurs moment by moment except when immediate payment is a condition, and the mode of its formation is that it is formed between the two contracting parties for the present, and it forms moment by moment in terms of legal effect, which is ownership according to the occurrence of the benefit. Its description: It is a binding contract if it is valid and free from options of condition, defect, and inspection. Whatever is valid as a price in sale, like currency, measured, or weighed items, is valid as a fee in ijarah, and whatever is invalid as a price is also invalid as a fee, like things such as slaves and garments. This is explicitly stated in al-Kafi and others. As for its conditions, they are of types: conditions of formation, conditions of effectiveness, conditions of validity, and conditions of bindingness. As for conditions of formation: 1) Among them is sanity, so that it does not form from a mad person or an unintelligent child, and puberty is not among its conditions, so that if an intelligent child hires out his property or himself, if he is authorized, it is effective, otherwise it is suspended until the guardian's permission. Islam is also not a condition at all, so hiring and being hired by a Muslim, dhimmi, harbi, or musta'min is valid. The contracting party being willing and deliberate is also not a condition for formation or effectiveness, but it is a condition for validity. 2) Among them is ownership and guardianship, so that the hiring by a fuduli (unauthorized person) is not effective due to lack of ownership and guardianship, but it is formed as suspended pending the owner's permission according to us. 3) Among them is the existence of the subject matter, so that if a fuduli hires out and the owner ratifies the contract after the benefit has been received, his hiring is not valid, and the fee goes to the contracting party because the contracted benefits have ceased to exist; but the hiring by an agent proceeds because guardianship exists. 4) Among them is delivery to the hirer in hiring of houses and the like if the contract is absolute from the condition of immediate payment according to us. 5) Among them is that the contract be absolute from the condition of option; if it contains an option, it is not effective during the option period. As for conditions of validity: 1) Among them is the consent of the contracting parties. 2) Among them is that the subject matter, which is the benefits, be known with a knowledge that prevents dispute; if it is unknown with an ignorance that leads to dispute, it prevents the validity of the contract, otherwise not. 3) Among them is specifying the location of the benefit, such that if he says 'I hire out to you one of these two houses,' it is not valid. 4) Among them is specifying the term in hiring houses, dwellings, etc., and in hiring a wet nurse.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on ijarah (hiring/leasing) in the Hanafi legal tradition. It begins by explaining why the chapter on ijarah is placed after the chapter on gift (hibah) and before the discussion of sadaqah. The author notes that ijarah resembles gift in being a transfer of ownership, but while gift transfers a physical thing ('ayn), ijarah transfers a benefit (manfa'ah). Since things are stronger and prior in existence, the chapter on ijarah is delayed. Additionally, ijarah shares with sadaqah the characteristic of being binding (lazim), so it comes after sadaqah. The linguistic definition of ijarah is given as a term for the fee (ajr) or reward for good work. The legal definition, as stated by the author of al-Wiqayah, is that it is a type of sale (a sale of benefits). The pillars (rukn) are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) using specific words, which are detailed in larger works. Ijarah is divided into two types: (1) hiring the benefits of tangible assets, such as houses, land, and animals, and (2) hiring labor, such as launders and tailors. The ruling (hukm) is that ownership of both the fee and the benefit is transferred moment by moment (saa'atan fa saa'atan) unless the fee is required immediately. The contract is formed at the moment of agreement, and legal ownership of the benefit accrues as the benefit occurs. The description (sifah) is that a valid ijarah contract is binding (lazim) if free from options of condition, defect, and inspection. Anything valid as a price in sale is also valid as a fee in ijarah, and anything invalid as a price is invalid as a fee; thus slaves and garments are not valid as fees because they are not generally used as prices. The conditions (shurut) of ijarah are classified into four types: conditions of formation (in'iqad), conditions of effectiveness (nafadh), conditions of validity (sihhah), and conditions of bindingness (luzum). The passage then details the conditions of formation. First, sanity ('aql) is required; the contract does not form from a mad person or a child lacking understanding. However, puberty is not a condition; an intelligent child can form a contract, but if not authorized, it is suspended pending guardian approval. Islam is not a condition; ijarah is valid with Muslims, dhimmis, harbis, and musta'mins. Free will and intent are not conditions for formation or effectiveness, but are required for validity. Second, ownership and guardianship are required; a fuduli (unauthorized person) cannot make the contract effective, but it can be suspended pending ratification. Third, the subject matter (the benefits) must exist at the time of contract; if a fuduli hires out and the owner ratifies after benefits are fully used, the contract is invalid because the benefits are gone; the fee goes to the fuduli. In contrast, an agent's contract is valid due to guardianship. Fourth, delivery (taslim) of the subject matter to the hirer is a condition for formation in contracts like house rental when the contract is absolute (without a condition of immediate payment). Fifth, the contract must be free from a condition of option (khiyar); if an option exists, the contract is not effective during the option period. The passage then begins to list conditions of validity. First, mutual consent of the parties. Second, the benefits must be known in a way that prevents dispute; ignorance leading to dispute invalidates the contract. Third, the specific location of the benefit must be identified; saying 'I hire out one of these two houses' is invalid due to ambiguity. Fourth, the term (muddah) must be specified for hiring houses and dwellings, and for hiring a wet nurse. The passage cuts off at this point. Hanafi scholars emphasize that these conditions ensure the contract avoids gharar (uncertainty) and is valid.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ījārah): A contract for the transfer of the right to use a benefit (manfa‘ah) in exchange for a fee (ajr).
المنفعة (al-manfa‘ah): The benefit or usufruct derived from a thing or from a person’s labor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal definition of ijarah in the Hanafi school?
A) A gift of benefit.
B) A sale of benefits.
C) A contract of partnership.
D) A gratuitous loan.

Key Takeaway
Ijarah is a binding sale of benefits, requiring a defined fee and defined benefit, with ownership passing moment by moment.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease

8 min
Conditions of Ijarah: Formation and Validity
Lists and explains the conditions of formation (in'iqad) and begins conditions of validity (sihhah) for ijarah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of formation (in'iqad) and validity (sihhah) for an ijarah contract according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish between the four categories of conditions.

Core Idea
The validity of an ijarah contract requires certain knowledge of the benefit and term; formation requires capacity and existence of the subject matter.

Now that we know ijarah is a sale of benefits, we must examine its conditions. Hanafi jurists classify conditions into four categories: conditions of formation (in'iqad), effectiveness (nafadh), validity (sihhah), and bindingness (luzum). This lesson covers the conditions of formation and the beginning of conditions of validity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fourfold classification of conditions
The author lists four types: shurut al-in'iqad (conditions for the contract to exist legally), shurut al-nafadh (conditions for it to take effect), shurut al-sihhah (conditions for it to be valid), and shurut al-luzum (conditions for it to be binding).
This classification is a hallmark of Hanafi contract theory. Other schools may not distinguish all four.
2
Examine conditions of formation (shurut al-in'iqad)
The passage details five conditions: sanity ('aql), ownership/guardianship (milk/wilaya), existence of the subject matter (qiyam al-ma'qud alayh), delivery (taslim) when the contract is absolute, and absence of a condition of option (khiyar).
Puberty, Islam, and free will are not conditions for formation; they pertain to effectiveness or validity.
3
Analyze the condition of sanity and capacity
The contract does not form from a mad person or a child without discernment. But an intelligent child's contract is suspended (mawquf) if not authorized, meaning it can be ratified later.
This shows the Hanafi concept of 'suspended' contract (mawquf) for the fuduli or unauthorized child.
4
Understand the role of ownership and guardianship
A fuduli (unauthorized person) cannot make the contract effective, but it is formed as suspended. If the owner ratifies after benefits are consumed, ratification is invalid because the subject matter (benefits) no longer exists.
Existence of the subject matter at the time of ratification is required; if benefits are gone, the contract cannot be validated.
5
Identify conditions of validity (shurut al-sihhah)
The passage lists: mutual consent (rida), knowledge of the benefit to avoid dispute, specification of location (mahall al-manfa'ah), and specification of term (muddah) in hiring houses and wet nurses.
These conditions prevent gharar (uncertainty) and ensure the contract is fair and enforceable.

لَمَّا كانت الهبةُ تشارك الإجارة في معنى التمليك، وكانت الهبة تمليك عين، والإجارة تمليك منفعة أَخَّرَ الإجارة عن الهبة لكون العين أقوى، وأيضاً الأعيان مقدَّمة وجوداً على المنافع، وأيضاً الأصل في الهبة عدم العوض، وفي الإجارة العوض، والعدم مقدم على الوجود، ثمّ لعقد الإجارة مناسبة خاصة ببيان الصدقة من حيث إنهما يقعان لازمين، فلذلك أورد «كتاب الإجارة» متصلاً بذكرِ الصدقة.
والإجارة لغة اسم للأجرة، وما هو يستحق على عمل الخير، ألا ترى أنه يقال : أعظم الله أجرك.
وشرعاً ما ذكره المصنف بقوله : وهي بيع يقع... الخ.
وركنها : هو الإيجاب والقبول بالألفاظ الموضوعة في عقد الإجارة، وتفصيلهما مذكور في المبسوطات. وهي على نوعين :
١. نوع يرد على منافع الأعيان ؛ كاستئجار الدار والأراضي والدواب ونحوها. ونوع يردّ على العمل : كاستئجار المحترفين للأعمال ؛ كالقصارة والخياطة ونحوهما. وحكمها : وقوع الملكِ في البدلين ساعة فساعة إلا بشرط تعجيل الإجارة، وكيفية انعقادها أنها تنعقد فيما بين المتعاقدين للحال، وتنعقد ساعة فساعة في حق الحكم، وهو الملك على حسب حدوث المنفعة.
وصفتها : أنها عقد لازم إن كانت صحيحة عرية عن خيار الشرط والعيب والرؤية، وما صح أن يكون ثمناً في البيع : كالنقود والمكيل والموزون صح أن يكون أجرةً في الإجارة، وما لا يصح ثمناً صلح أجرةً أيضاً : كالأعيان مثل العبيد والثياب. صرح به في «الكافي»، وغيره.
وأما شرائطها فهي على أنواع شرائط الانعقاد، وشرائط النفاذ، وشرائط الصحة، وشرائط اللزوم.
:
أما شرائط الانعقاد :
١. فمنها : العقل حتى لا تنعقد من المجنون والصبي الذي لا يعقل، والبلوغ ليس من شرائطها حتى أنّ الصبي العاقل لو أجر ماله أو نفسه فإن كان مأذوناً تنفذ، وإلا تقف على إجازة الولي.
والإسلام أيضاً ليس بشرط أصلاً حتى يجوز الإجارة والاستئجار من المسلم والذمي والحربي والمستأمن.
وكون العاقد طائعاً عامداً أيضاً ليس بشرط للانعقاد ولا للنفاذ، لكنه شرط الصحة.
٢. ومنها : الملك والولاية حتى لا تنفذ إجارة الفضولي ؛ لعدم الملك والولاية، لكنه ينعقد موقوفاً على إجازة المالك عندنا.
٣. ومنها : قيام المعقود عليه، حتى إذا أجر الفضولي فأجاز المالك العقد بعد استيفاء المنفعة لم تجز إجارته، وكانت الأجرة للعاقد ؛ لأنَّ المنافع المعقود عليها قد انعدمت، وإجارة الوكيل بأخذه لوجود الولاية.
٤. ومنها: تسليم المستأجر في إجارة المنازل ونحوها إذا كان العقد مطلقاً عن شرط التعجيل عندنا.
٥. ومنها : أن يكون العقد مطلقاً عن شرط الخيار، فإن كان فيه خيار لا تنفذ في مدة الخيار.
وأما شرائط الصحة :
١. فمنها : رضاء المتعاقدين.
٢. ومنها : أن يكون المعقود عليه وهو المنافع معلوماً علماً يمنع المنازعة، فإن كان مجهولاً جهالة تفضي إلى المنازعة يمنع صحة العقد، وإلا فلا.
٣. ومنها : محل بيان المنفعة، حتى لو قال : أجرتُك إحدى هاتين الدارين لا تصح.
٤. ومنها : بيانُ المدَّة في الدور المنازل ونحوها وفي استئجار الظئر.
Book of Ijarah (Hiring). Since gift shares with ijarah in the meaning of ownership, and gift is ownership of a thing, while ijarah is ownership of benefit, he delayed ijarah after gift because a thing is stronger, and also things are prior in existence to benefits, and also the basis in gift is absence of consideration, while in ijarah is consideration, and absence is prior to existence. Then the contract of ijarah has a specific connection to the exposition of sadaqah because both are binding contracts, therefore he introduced the 'Book of Ijarah' connected to the mention of sadaqah. Ijarah linguistically is a name for the fee, and for what is due for good work; do you not see that it is said: 'May Allah increase your reward (ajr).' Legally, it is what the author mentioned: 'It is a sale that occurs...' etc. Its pillar is the offer and acceptance with the words established in the contract of ijarah, and their detail is mentioned in the comprehensive works. It is of two types: 1) A type that pertains to benefits of things, like hiring a house, land, animals, etc. And a type that pertains to work, like hiring professionals for works such as laundering, tailoring, etc. Its ruling: Ownership of both countervalues occurs moment by moment except when immediate payment is a condition, and the mode of its formation is that it is formed between the two contracting parties for the present, and it forms moment by moment in terms of legal effect, which is ownership according to the occurrence of the benefit. Its description: It is a binding contract if it is valid and free from options of condition, defect, and inspection. Whatever is valid as a price in sale, like currency, measured, or weighed items, is valid as a fee in ijarah, and whatever is invalid as a price is also invalid as a fee, like things such as slaves and garments. This is explicitly stated in al-Kafi and others. As for its conditions, they are of types: conditions of formation, conditions of effectiveness, conditions of validity, and conditions of bindingness. As for conditions of formation: 1) Among them is sanity, so that it does not form from a mad person or an unintelligent child, and puberty is not among its conditions, so that if an intelligent child hires out his property or himself, if he is authorized, it is effective, otherwise it is suspended until the guardian's permission. Islam is also not a condition at all, so hiring and being hired by a Muslim, dhimmi, harbi, or musta'min is valid. The contracting party being willing and deliberate is also not a condition for formation or effectiveness, but it is a condition for validity. 2) Among them is ownership and guardianship, so that the hiring by a fuduli (unauthorized person) is not effective due to lack of ownership and guardianship, but it is formed as suspended pending the owner's permission according to us. 3) Among them is the existence of the subject matter, so that if a fuduli hires out and the owner ratifies the contract after the benefit has been received, his hiring is not valid, and the fee goes to the contracting party because the contracted benefits have ceased to exist; but the hiring by an agent proceeds because guardianship exists. 4) Among them is delivery to the hirer in hiring of houses and the like if the contract is absolute from the condition of immediate payment according to us. 5) Among them is that the contract be absolute from the condition of option; if it contains an option, it is not effective during the option period. As for conditions of validity: 1) Among them is the consent of the contracting parties. 2) Among them is that the subject matter, which is the benefits, be known with a knowledge that prevents dispute; if it is unknown with an ignorance that leads to dispute, it prevents the validity of the contract, otherwise not. 3) Among them is specifying the location of the benefit, such that if he says 'I hire out to you one of these two houses,' it is not valid. 4) Among them is specifying the term in hiring houses, dwellings, etc., and in hiring a wet nurse.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on ijarah (hiring/leasing) in the Hanafi legal tradition. It begins by explaining why the chapter on ijarah is placed after the chapter on gift (hibah) and before the discussion of sadaqah. The author notes that ijarah resembles gift in being a transfer of ownership, but while gift transfers a physical thing ('ayn), ijarah transfers a benefit (manfa'ah). Since things are stronger and prior in existence, the chapter on ijarah is delayed. Additionally, ijarah shares with sadaqah the characteristic of being binding (lazim), so it comes after sadaqah. The linguistic definition of ijarah is given as a term for the fee (ajr) or reward for good work. The legal definition, as stated by the author of al-Wiqayah, is that it is a type of sale (a sale of benefits). The pillars (rukn) are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) using specific words, which are detailed in larger works. Ijarah is divided into two types: (1) hiring the benefits of tangible assets, such as houses, land, and animals, and (2) hiring labor, such as launders and tailors. The ruling (hukm) is that ownership of both the fee and the benefit is transferred moment by moment (saa'atan fa saa'atan) unless the fee is required immediately. The contract is formed at the moment of agreement, and legal ownership of the benefit accrues as the benefit occurs. The description (sifah) is that a valid ijarah contract is binding (lazim) if free from options of condition, defect, and inspection. Anything valid as a price in sale is also valid as a fee in ijarah, and anything invalid as a price is invalid as a fee; thus slaves and garments are not valid as fees because they are not generally used as prices. The conditions (shurut) of ijarah are classified into four types: conditions of formation (in'iqad), conditions of effectiveness (nafadh), conditions of validity (sihhah), and conditions of bindingness (luzum). The passage then details the conditions of formation. First, sanity ('aql) is required; the contract does not form from a mad person or a child lacking understanding. However, puberty is not a condition; an intelligent child can form a contract, but if not authorized, it is suspended pending guardian approval. Islam is not a condition; ijarah is valid with Muslims, dhimmis, harbis, and musta'mins. Free will and intent are not conditions for formation or effectiveness, but are required for validity. Second, ownership and guardianship are required; a fuduli (unauthorized person) cannot make the contract effective, but it can be suspended pending ratification. Third, the subject matter (the benefits) must exist at the time of contract; if a fuduli hires out and the owner ratifies after benefits are fully used, the contract is invalid because the benefits are gone; the fee goes to the fuduli. In contrast, an agent's contract is valid due to guardianship. Fourth, delivery (taslim) of the subject matter to the hirer is a condition for formation in contracts like house rental when the contract is absolute (without a condition of immediate payment). Fifth, the contract must be free from a condition of option (khiyar); if an option exists, the contract is not effective during the option period. The passage then begins to list conditions of validity. First, mutual consent of the parties. Second, the benefits must be known in a way that prevents dispute; ignorance leading to dispute invalidates the contract. Third, the specific location of the benefit must be identified; saying 'I hire out one of these two houses' is invalid due to ambiguity. Fourth, the term (muddah) must be specified for hiring houses and dwellings, and for hiring a wet nurse. The passage cuts off at this point. Hanafi scholars emphasize that these conditions ensure the contract avoids gharar (uncertainty) and is valid.

Key Terms
شرائط الانعقاد (shurūṭ al-in‘iqād): Conditions necessary for the contract to be formed (exist legally), but may be suspended for effectiveness.
الفضولي (al-fuḍūlī): A person who acts without authority or ownership, making a contract on behalf of another without permission.
جهالة (jahālah): Uncertainty or ignorance about a contractual element that leads to dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for the formation (in'iqad) of an ijarah contract according to the passage?
A) The contracting party must be sane.
B) The contracting party must be a Muslim.
C) The subject matter must exist at the time of contract.
D) The contract must be free from a condition of option.

Key Takeaway
An ijarah contract is only valid if its subject matter (benefit) is known and the term is specified; formation requires capacity and existence of the benefit at the time of contract.

Conditions of Formation (In'aqad) in Ijarah
Enumeration of formation conditions for Ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the five conditions required for the formation (in'aqad) of an Ijarah contract according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
An Ijarah contract is formed only when the parties are sane, the lessor has ownership, the usufruct exists, delivery is immediate, and no option condition suspends it.

In this passage, the author begins the detailed discussion of Ijarah by enumerating the conditions for its formation (shurut al-in'aqad). These are the prerequisites without which the contract does not come into existence at all. Understanding these conditions is essential for any valid lease or hire arrangement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five formation conditions
Read the Arabic text and list each condition mentioned. Note the key Arabic terms for each.
The text uses 'منها' to list each condition.
2
Analyze the first condition: Sanity
The contract is not formed from an insane person or a child who lacks comprehension. Puberty is not required, but a discerning minor's lease is valid if authorized.
Contrast with other contracts where puberty might be required.
3
Analyze the second condition: Ownership and Authority
The lessor must own the usufruct or have the authority to lease. A fuduli's lease is suspended pending ratification.
Fuduli is an unauthorized agent.
4
Analyze the third and fourth conditions: Existence of usufruct and immediate delivery
The usufruct must be capable of existence at the time of contract. Additionally, in absolute leases of houses, delivery must be immediate.
If usufruct is already consumed, ratification is impossible.
5
Analyze the fifth condition: No option condition
The contract must be free from any option (khiyar) that suspends the contract. If an option period exists, the contract is not effective during that period.
This condition is specific to formation; options may affect bindingness later.
