Validity of Ijarah with Extremely Long Duration
Explains how usufruct is known by duration and discusses validity of very long durations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ijarah contracts with durations exceeding normal lifespan and explain the proof based on the wording principle.

Core Idea
In ijarah, the wording rules: even a duration beyond normal life expectancy is valid because it is time-limited in wording.

How do we know the usufruct in an ijarah contract? One method is by specifying the duration – e.g., leasing a house for one year. But what if the duration is so long that the parties will likely not live to see its end? Is such a contract valid? The Hanafi school addresses this, and the preferred view upholds validity, focusing on the wording of the contract rather than the statistical outcome. This lesson unpacks the proof and the intra-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue: how usufruct is known in ijarah
The passage explains that usufruct can be known by specifying a duration (e.g., for dwelling or cultivation). The question is whether a duration that is extremely long (beyond normal lifespan) still makes the usufruct known and the contract valid.
The key term is muddah (duration) – it must be known (ma'lum) but there is no maximum bound.
2
State the Hanafi position on very long durations
The chosen view (mufta bihi) is that the contract is valid (sahih) even if the duration exceeds typical life expectancy. The author (musannif) supports this.
This is based on the principle 'al-'ibrah lil-lafz' – consideration is given to the wording.
3
Understand the proof: analogy to a hundred-year marriage
The author draws an analogy: just as a marriage contract for a hundred years is a temporary marriage (mut'ah, though in Sunni fiqh mut'ah is invalid, but here used as a legal hypothetical to show time-limiting), so too an ijarah for a very long period is time-limited, not perpetual. The wording 'to one hundred years' implies a term, not perpetuity.
The proof is qiyas (analogy) based on the shared illah of tawqit (time limitation).
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars, notably Qadi Abu 'Ismat al-Amiri, held that if the duration is such that the parties would not normally live to see it, the contract is invalid – treating the dominant expectation (ghalib) as certainty (mutayaqqan), thus resembling perpetual ijarah which is invalid.
This view is presented as 'qila' (it was said) and is not the relied-upon position.
5
Apply the tarjih (resolution)
The author chooses the majority view (validity) and indicates that the fatwa is on it. The operative ruling for fatwa today is that such a contract is valid.
The tarjih authority is the author (musannif) himself; the authoritative weight is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ويعلم...الخ ; يعني ويعلم المنفعة تارة ببيان المدة كإجارة الدار للسكنى، وإجارة الأرض للزراعة، فتصح إجارتها مدة معلومة سواء كانت تلك المدة طويلة أو قصيرة ; لأنّ المدّة إذا كانت معلومة كانت المنفعة معلومة فأفاد أنها تجوز، ولو كانت المدة لا يعيش إلى مثلها عادة. واختاره المصنف ; لأنَّ العبرة للفظ ، وأنه يقتضي التوقيت كما إذا تزوج امرأةً إلى مئة سنة فإنّه توقيت لا تأبيد حتى يكون متعة، وإن كانت هذه المدة لا يعيش إليها أحدهما غالباً اعتباراً للفظ ، وقيل : لو ذكر مدة لا يعيش إليها أحدهما غالباً لا تصح ، وبه أفتى القاضي أبو عصمة العامري ) ; لأن الغالب كالمتيقن في حق الأحكام، فكانت كالإجارة المؤبدة.
His saying: 'And it is known...etc.' means: the usufruct is known sometimes by specifying the duration, like leasing a house for dwelling, or land for cultivation for a specified period, be it long or short. So it is valid to lease it for a known period, even if the duration is one that one would not normally live to see. The author (musannif) chose this view because the consideration is given to the wording, and that it requires a time limit (tawqit) just as if one marries a woman for a hundred years, it is a time limit, not perpetual, so that it is mut'ah (temporary marriage), even if this duration is such that one of them would not normally live to see it, consideration is given to the wording. And it was said: if he mentions a period that one would not normally live to see, it is not valid, and this was the fatwa of Qadi Abu 'Ismat al-Amiri, because the usual (ghalib) is like certainty (mutayaqqan) in the context of legal rulings, so it is like a perpetual ijarah.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing how the usufruct is known in an ijarah contract: by specifying the duration. The author supports the view that even if the duration is extremely long beyond normal life expectancy, the contract is valid, considering the wording and analogy to a marriage for a hundred years (which is a temporary marriage). The opposing view of Qadi Abu 'Ismat al-Amiri says such contracts are invalid, treating the dominant expectation as certainty.

Key Terms
مدة (muddah): The specified duration of the ijarah contract, which must be known (ma'lum) and can be any length, even beyond typical lifespan.
توقيت (tawqit): Time-limiting or specifying a duration, as opposed to ta'bid (perpetuity). The contract is considered mu'aqqat (temporary).
العبرة للفظ (al-’ibrah lil-lafz): Consideration is given to the wording (of the contract), not to external assumptions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is invalid because the usufruct cannot be known
B) It is valid because consideration is given to the wording
C) It is valid only if the lessee is likely to survive
D) It is invalid because it resembles perpetual ijarah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, a specified duration – no matter how long – makes the usufruct known and the contract valid, because the wording of the time limit controls over the likelihood of survival.

Duration in Ijarah: Specifying Benefit in Long-Term Leases
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of ijarah with improbably long duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of an ijarah with an extremely long duration and distinguish the two intra-Hanafi positions on the issue.

Core Idea
A lease is valid for any specified duration, even if improbably long, because consideration is given to the wording, not the lifespan.

In ijarah, the benefit (manfa'ah) must be known. One way to know it is by specifying the duration. But what if the duration is so long that neither party is expected to live? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents an intra-Hanafi debate: the author upholds validity based on the wording, while Qadi Abu 'Ismah invalidates it based on normal expectation. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the core legal question: Is an ijarah valid when the specified duration exceeds the normal lifespan of the parties?
2
State the preferred Hanafi position
The author holds the lease valid because the duration is specified and the benefit is known. The key principle is al-'ibrah li al-lafz (consideration is given to the wording).
3
Understand the proof
The author draws an analogy with a marriage contract for a fixed term of 100 years. Such a marriage is considered a valid tawqit (fixed-term), not ta'bid (perpetual), even if the parties won't live that long.
This analogy is qiyas: the 'illa is the specification of a term (tawqit).
4
Present the opposing view
Qadi Abu 'Ismah argues that the lease is invalid because the norm (ghalab) is treated as certainty in legal rulings. An improbable term makes the contract effectively perpetual (ijarah mu'abbadah), which is invalid.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the preferred view, contracts like 99-year leases are valid; under the opposing view, they would be void. The mufta bihi is the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة/تعريفها وشروطها وصورها كذلك دين أو عين، ويُعْلَمُ النفعُ : بذِكْرِ المدَّةِ كسكنى الدار، وزراعة الأرض مدة، كذا طالت أو قصرت فإنه ذكر في «الكتاب»: إنه لو قال : وهبتُ منك منفعة هذه الدار شهراً بدرهم جاز، وإنما لا يجوز إذا أضاف البيع إلى منفعة الدار ; لأنَّ الإجارة لا تنعقد بلفظ البيع. انتهى وقال صاحب «البحر»: «إضافةُ الإجارة إلى منافع الدار جائزة». انتهى. قوله : ويعلم...الخ ; يعني ويعلم المنفعة تارة ببيان المدة كإجارة الدار للسكنى، وإجارة الأرض للزراعة، فتصح إجارتها مدة معلومة سواء كانت تلك المدة طويلة أو قصيرة ; لأنّ المدّة إذا كانت معلومة كانت المنفعة معلومة فأفاد أنها تجوز، ولو كانت المدة لا يعيش إلى مثلها عادة. واختاره المصنف ; لأنَّ العبرة للفظ ، وأنه يقتضي التوقيت كما إذا تزوج امرأةً إلى مئة سنة فإنّه توقيت لا تأبيد حتى يكون متعة، وإن كانت هذه المدة لا يعيش إليها أحدهما غالباً اعتباراً للفظ ، وقيل : لو ذكر مدة لا يعيش إليها أحدهما غالباً لا تصح ، وبه أفتى القاضي أبو عصمة العامري ; لأن الغالب كالمتيقن في حق الأحكام، فكانت كالإجارة المؤبدة.
Book of Ijarah / Its definition, conditions and forms. Similarly, a debt or a specific object. And the benefit is known by mentioning the duration, such as the residence of a house, and the cultivation of land for a period, whether long or short. It is mentioned in al-Kitab: If he said, 'I gifted you the benefit of this house for a month for a dirham,' it is valid, but it is not valid if he attaches the sale to the benefit of the house because ijarah is not contracted with the word of sale. End. The author of al-Bahr said: 'Attaching ijarah to the benefits of the house is permissible.' End. His saying: 'And it is known... etc.' means: The benefit is sometimes known by specifying the duration, like renting a house for residence and land for cultivation. So, its lease for a known period is valid, whether that period is long or short, because when the duration is known, the benefit is known. So it indicates that it is permissible even if the period is such that one would not normally live to its like. The author chose this because consideration is given to the wording, and it necessitates a fixed term, just as if one marries a woman for a hundred years, it is a fixed term, not perpetual, so it is mut'ah (temporary marriage), even if neither would normally live that long, due to consideration of the wording. And it is said: If he mentions a duration such that neither would normally live to its end, it is not valid, and this is the fatwa of Qadi Abu 'Ismah al-'Amiri, because the norm is like certainty with respect to rulings, so it is like a perpetual lease.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rule that the benefit (manfa'ah) in an ijarah (lease) contract must be known. The text explains that one way to make the benefit known is by specifying the duration of the lease, such as renting a house for a month or land for a year. The duration can be long or short; even a period that an average person would not live to see (e.g., a hundred years) is valid according to the preferred Hanafi position. The author supports this by analogy with marriage: a marriage contract for a hundred years is still a fixed-term contract (tawqit), not perpetual. However, there is a different view attributed to Qadi Abu 'Ismah al-'Amiri that such an improbably long duration invalidates the lease, considering the norm as certainty in legal rulings, likening it to a perpetual lease which is not allowed in ijarah. The chosen view (mukhtar) is that the lease is valid as long as the duration is explicitly stated, regardless of how long it is.

Key Terms
منفعة (manfa'ah): The benefit or usufruct of an object, which is the subject matter of ijarah.
مدة (muddah): The period of time for which the benefit is leased.
إجارة مؤبدة (ijarah mu'abbadah): A lease that is perpetual, not fixed-term, which is invalid in ijarah.
توقيت (tawqit): The act of specifying a fixed term for a contract, making it temporary rather than perpetual.

D) The rent is paid in full in advance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school predominantly validates ijarah contracts with any specified duration, even if impossibly long, because the benefit is made known through explicit temporal boundaries.

Maximum Lease Duration for Waqf Property: Three Years
Ruling on maximum lease duration for waqf property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum lease duration for waqf property under the chosen Hanafi view and explain the rationale and intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
Waqf property cannot be leased beyond three years to prevent the lessee from claiming ownership.

Leasing waqf property requires special care to preserve the endowment's perpetuity. The chosen Hanafi view sets a three-year maximum lease duration, but not all Hanafi scholars agree. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its rationale, and the internal school dispute, equipping you with the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is leasing waqf property. Determine the hukm level for leases exceeding three years: according to the chosen view, it is not valid (la tasihh).
The ruling applies when the endower has not stipulated a shorter period.
2
Locate the rationale (ʿilla)
The text gives the reason: 'so that the lessee is not led to claim that it is his property.' This is a rational proof (dalil ʿaqli) based on protecting the waqf from usurpation.
This is not derived from a textual source but from the objective of preserving the endowment.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
The passage cites three positions: scholars of Balkh prohibit beyond three years unconditionally; other Hanafi scholars allow it if the lessee is trustworthy; Abu al-Layth recommends referring to a judge for annulment.
These are all valid Hanafi views, but the chosen position is the strictest for caution.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The author (ʿUmda al-Riʿāya) presents the three-year limit as the chosen view (mukhtār). This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
This is a later preferred view (fatawa mutakhkhirin), not the earliest Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن في الوقف لا تصح فوق ثلاث سنين في المختار ; كيلا يدعى المستأجِرُ أَنَّهُ ملكه ، فَعِلة عدم الجواز إذا كانت هذا المعنى لا يصح الإجارة الطويلة بعقود مختلفة، كما جوزها البعض. قوله : لكن... الخ ; ولكن في الوقف لا يجوز أن يزاد مدة الإجارة على ثلاث سنين ، قال قاضي خان له في «فتاواه»: «المتولي إذا أجر الوقف إن كان الواقف شرط أن لا يؤاجر أكثر من السنة، لا يجوز الإجارة أكثر من سنة، فإن لم يكن شرط ذلك تجوز الإجارة إلى ثلاث سنين، فإن أجرها أكثر من ذلك اختلفوا فيه : قال مشايخ بلخ: لا تجوز له إجارة الوقف أكثر من ثلاث سنين. قال مشايخنا : يجوز ذلك إذا كان المستأجر ممن لا يخاف منه دعوى الملك إذا طالت المدة. وقال الفقيه أبو الليث الله : الاحتياط في ذلك أن يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي حتى يبطله». انتهى
But regarding waqf, it is not valid beyond three years in the chosen view, so that the lessee is not led to claim that it is his property. So the reason for non-permissibility, if this meaning is intended, is that the long lease is not valid with different contracts, as some have allowed it. His saying: 'But... etc.' means: In waqf, the lease period should not be increased beyond three years. Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: 'If the trustee leases waqf property, if the endower stipulated that it should not be leased for more than a year, then leasing for more than a year is not permissible. If he did not stipulate that, then leasing up to three years is permissible. If he leases it for more than that, there is disagreement: The scholars of Balkh say: It is not permissible for him to lease waqf for more than three years. Our scholars say: It is permissible if the lessee is someone from whom no claim of ownership is feared if the period is long. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: The precaution is that the matter be referred to the judge so that he may annul it.' End.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific restriction on leasing waqf (endowment) property. The chosen (mukhtar) view in the Hanafi school is that a waqf property cannot be leased for more than three consecutive years. The reason is to prevent the lessee from claiming ownership of the property after a long period. The commentary cites Qadi Khan's Fatawa, which elaborates: if the endower stipulated a maximum lease period (e.g., one year), that must be followed. If no stipulation exists, leasing for up to three years is permissible. For longer periods, there is an ikhtilaf (disagreement) among Hanafi scholars: the scholars of Balkh prohibit any lease over three years, while other Hanafi scholars allow it if the lessee is trustworthy and unlikely to claim ownership. Abu al-Layth recommends referring the matter to a judge for annulment. The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is the three-year limit as the default precaution.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): An endowment of property for charitable purposes, where the corpus is inalienable.
مستأجر (musta'jir): The lessee who rents the waqf property.
إجارة (ijarah): Lease or hire contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Indefinitely

Key Takeaway
The chosen Hanafi ruling restricts waqf leases to a maximum of three years to safeguard the endowment from potential ownership claims.

Shafi'i Dispute on Maximum Duration of Ijarah and Hanafi Response
Inter-madhhab dispute on ijarah duration and Hanafi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the maximum duration of ijarah and evaluate the Shafi'i counterarguments based on the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
In ijarah, the benefit is known only by specifying a term—therefore, any fixed term is valid, and no maximum is needed.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute over whether ijarah contracts can be for an unlimited duration or only up to a certain limit. Imam al-Shafi'i had three narrations restricting the term, while the Hanafi school argues that once the term is specified, the benefit is known and the contract is valid. The reasoning hinges on how benefits are known—through duration—versus how physical objects are known in sale.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi stance: ijarah is valid for any specified term, with no maximum duration.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i has three different narrations: one-year limit, thirty-year limit, and no limit (by analogy with sale).
The existence of multiple narrations indicates ikhtilaf within the Shafi'i school itself.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i justifications: necessity (one year), custom (thirty years), and analogy with sale of goods (unlimited).
The third narration is similar to the Hanafi position, but the dominant positions restrict duration.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: the permissibility of ijarah depends on the benefit being known (ma'lumah). Benefits are not known like physical objects; they require a specified duration to be known. Hence, any specified term is sufficient, and there is no need for an arbitrary maximum.
The Hanafi response contrasts ijarah with sale: sale requires perpetuity (ta'bid), so setting a term invalidates sale; ijarah is inherently temporary.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis allow long-term leases (e.g., 99 years) while Shafi'is limit to 1 or 30 years in the dominant narrations.
This has significant implications for modern long-term leasing arrangements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشافعي له قدرها في رواية بسنة، فلا تجوز بأكثر منها ; لأن جوازها كان للضرورة، ولا ضرورة إلى أكثر منها. وفي رواية : بثلاثين سنة، فلا تجوز بأكثر منها ; لأنه قلما يستعمل بالأجرة أكثر من ذلك. وفي رواية : تجوز أبداً ; لأن بيع المنافع كبيع الأعيان. والجواب : أنّ المجوّز للإجارة كونها معلومة، فلا معنى للمبيع بعد أن صارت معلومة، والمنافع لا يكون معلومة إلا بضرب المدَّة لها ، فلا تجوز إلا مؤجلة، بخلاف بيع الأعيان، فإنّها معلومة بدونه، ومن شروطه التأبيد، حتى لو ضرب له أجلا فسد البيع.
Al-Shafi'i has a transmission that it (the lease) is limited to one year, so it is not permissible for more than that, because its permissibility was due to necessity, and there is no need for more than that. In another transmission: thirty years, so it is not permissible for more than that, because rarely is it used with rent for more than that. In another transmission: it is permissible forever, because selling benefits is like selling commodities. The response: That which makes ijarah permissible is that it is known (specified), so there is no meaning to that which is sold after it has become known, and benefits are not known except by specifying a duration for them, so they are not permissible except when fixed-term. Unlike the sale of commodities, for they are known without that, and one of its conditions is perpetuity, so if a term is set for it, the sale becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage presents the Shafi'i view on the maximum duration of an ijarah contract and the Hanafi response. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, there are three narrations: (1) the lease can only be for one year, based on the idea that ijarah is a concession (rukhsah) for necessity, and one year suffices; (2) thirty years, because it is uncommon to rent property for longer; (3) indefinitely, because selling benefits is like selling physical objects (which can be sold forever). The Hanafi response (jawab) argues that ijarah is valid because the benefit is made known (ma'lum) through specifying a duration. Benefits are not inherently known like physical objects; they require a time limit to be specified. Therefore, ijarah must always be fixed-term (mu'ajjalah). In contrast, selling a physical object does not require a term and is perpetual by nature; setting a term would invalidate the sale. Hence, there is no need to impose an arbitrary maximum duration in ijarah; as long as the term is specified, the contract is valid. This response refutes the Shafi'i restrictions by focusing on the essential condition of ijarah: the benefit must be known, which is achieved by any specified duration, not by limiting the length.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): A contract for the benefit of an object in exchange for rent, valid only for a specified term.
بيع المنافع (bay' al-manafi'): Sale of benefits/usufructs, which some Shafi'is analogize to sale of physical objects.
تأبيد (ta'bid): Perpetuity; a condition in sale that the ownership is permanent, unlike ijarah which is temporary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi argument against limiting the duration of an ijarah contract to a specific maximum?
A) Ijarah is a form of sale and thus can be perpetual.
B) The benefit in ijarah is only known by specifying a duration, so any specified term is valid.
C) Necessity requires long leases for large properties.
D) The Quran commands no restrictions on lease duration.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ijarah contracts are valid for any specified duration because the benefit is known through the term; no maximum limit exists.

Leasing Waqf Property: The Three-Year Limit
States Hanafi ruling and refutes Shafi'i views on lease duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on waqf lease duration, explain the rationale, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterarguments.

Core Idea
A waqf lease beyond three years is invalid to prevent ownership claims.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: how long can a waqf property be leased? The Hanafi school holds a firm limit of three years, grounded in the concern that longer possession may lead to the lessee claiming ownership. You will learn the ruling, its reasoning, and how the Hanafi response refutes the Shafi'i positions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that a waqf lease exceeding three years is not valid (la tasihh). This is the mufta bihi position.
The passage says 'fi al-mukhtar'.
2
Identify the opponent and their views
Imam al-Shafi'i has three narrated views: one year, thirty years, and perpetuity.
Each view is based on a different rationale.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Each Shafi'i view has a rational basis: necessity, rare use beyond 30 years, analogy to sale of tangibles.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi refutation: The permissibility of ijarah depends on the subject being known (ma'lum). Benefits become known only by specifying a term; therefore a lease must have a fixed term. Selling a tangible asset does not require a term for knowledge; indeed, a sale must be perpetual.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In Hanafi law, a waqf lease for more than three years is fasid (invalid). In Shafi'i law, depending on the narration, it could be valid for a longer period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
لكن في الوقف لا تصح فوق ثلاث سنين في المختار) ؛ كيلا يدعى المستأجِرُ أَنَّهُ ملكه ، فَعِلة عدم الجواز إذا كانت هذا المعنى لا يصح الإجارة الطويلة بعقود مختلفة، كما جوزها البعض
والشافعي له قدرها في رواية بسنة، فلا تجوز بأكثر منها ؛ لأن جوازها كان للضرورة، ولا ضرورة إلى أكثر منها.
وفي رواية : بثلاثين سنة، فلا تجوز بأكثر منها ؛ لأنه قلما يستعمل بالأجرة أكثر من ذلك.
وفي رواية : تجوز أبداً ؛ لأن بيع المنافع كبيع الأعيان.
والجواب : أنّ المجوّز للإجارة كونها معلومة، فلا معنى للمبيع بعد أن صارت معلومة، والمنافع لا يكون معلومة إلا بضرب المدَّة لها ، فلا تجوز إلا مؤجلة، بخلاف بيع الأعيان، فإنّها معلومة بدونه، ومن شروطه التأبيد، حتى لو ضرب لـه أجلا فسد البيع
Summary of the final for the Umdat al-Ri'aya: However, in waqf, the lease is not valid for more than three years in the selected opinion; so that the lessee does not claim it as his property. The reason for non-permissibility, if this meaning is considered, renders a long-term lease invalid even with multiple contracts, as some have permitted it. Al-Shafi'i has its duration in one narration as one year, so it is not permissible for more than that, because its permissibility was due to necessity, and there is no necessity for more. In another narration: thirty years, so it is not permissible for more, because rarely is property used for rent beyond that. In another narration: it is permissible forever, because selling benefits is like selling tangible assets. The answer: The permissibility of a lease is due to it being known; the sold item has no meaning after it becomes known, and benefits are only known by fixing a term for them, so it is only permissible with a fixed term, unlike selling tangible assets, which are known without it, and one of its conditions is perpetuity, so if a term is set, the sale becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the maximum duration for leasing waqf property. The Hanafi position, stated as 'al-mukhtar', is that a waqf lease cannot exceed three years. The rationale is to prevent the lessee from claiming ownership through long possession. The commentator notes that if the underlying reason is this, then even multiple consecutive contracts would be invalid, contrary to some who permit it. The matn then presents three narrated views of Imam al-Shafi'i: one year, thirty years, and perpetuity. The Hanafi response argues that permissibility of a lease depends on the subject matter being known (ma'lum). Benefits (manafi') become known only by defining a term; thus a lease must be for a fixed term. In contrast, selling a tangible asset ('ayn) does not require a term for knowledge; in fact, a sale contract requires perpetuity, and fixing a term invalidates it. This distinction refutes the Shafi'i view that compares lease to sale.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment property that cannot be sold or owned, but leased for benefit.
الإجارة (al-ijarah): Lease of a specific benefit for a known term and rent.
المنافع (al-manafi): Benefits (usufruct) derived from property; the subject of a lease contract.
العين (al-'ayn): Tangible asset; physical property that can be sold without a term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mukhtar position, what is the maximum lease term for waqf property?
A) One year
B) Three years
C) Thirty years
D) Perpetuity

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a waqf lease exceeding three years is invalid (fasid) to prevent the lessee from claiming ownership.

Legal Stratagem for Long-Term Waqf Lease
Explains a valid hila for leasing waqf for extended periods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mechanism of the Hanafi hila for long-term waqf lease and identify the conditions under which it is valid.

Core Idea
Multiple separate one-year contracts make a long waqf lease permissible while preserving legal form.

The Hanafi school developed a clever legal stratagem (hila) to overcome the restriction on leasing waqf property for more than three years. This lesson unpacks the mechanism from the works of later Hanafi masters, showing how multiple one-year contracts create a binding first term and non-binding subsequent terms, giving the mutawalli flexibility to protect the waqf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Waqf leases are generally limited to three years. A longer single contract would be invalid. The hila solves this.
The base rule: ijara on waqf cannot exceed three years in Hanafi law.
2
Describe the hila structure
The mutawalli executes multiple contracts, each for one year (e.g., 30 contracts). Only the first contract is binding at the time of signing.
The later contracts are considered 'future' (mudaf) and thus non-binding.
3
Explain legal effect
The first contract is lazim (binding); the rest are ghayr lazim (non-binding) because they are deferred. The mutawalli retains the right to cancel the non-binding ones.
This protects the waqf if the lessee might claim ownership over time.
4
Note the condition
The hila works only if the mutawalli cancels the non-binding contracts when necessary (e.g., fear of waqf loss). The text states: 'the custodian may cancel the non-binding contracts if he fears the waqf will be lost.'
This is a condition for permissibility, not an automatic void.
5
Recognize the ikhtilaf
Sadr al-Islam preferred obtaining judicial authorization; al-Sadr al-Shahid differentiated between agricultural land (up to three years) and other properties (one year), subject to maslaha.
The mufta bihi position accepts the multiple-contract hila, but with judicial oversight recommended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في شرح حيل الخصاف: الحيلة بجواز الإجارة على الأوقاف أن يعقد عقوداً متفرقة على الأوقاف كل عقد على سنة، فيكتب: استأجر فلان بن فلان كذا، بثلاثين عقداً، كل عقد على سنة، فيكون العقد الأول لازماً والثاني غير لازم؛ لأنه مضاف ذكره في الكفاية نقلاً عن الذخيرة. فلمتولي الوقف أن يفسح الإجارة في العقود الغير اللازمة إذا خاف بطلان الوقف لدعوى الملك بمرور الزمان، فعلى هذا اندفع اعتراض الشارح. وذكر صدر الإسلام له: إن الحيلة فيه أن يرفع إلى الحاكم حتى يجيزه، والصدر الشهيد له كان يفتي بالجواز في ثلاث سنين في الضياع، إلا إذا كانت المصلحة في المنع، وفي غير الضياع بعدم الجواز فيما زاد على سنة واحدة، إلا إذا كانت المصلحة في الجواز.
In Sharh Hiyal al-Khassaf: The stratagem for permissibility of leasing waqf properties is to make multiple separate contracts on the waqf, each contract for one year. He writes: 'So-and-so leased such-and-such with thirty contracts, each contract for one year.' Then the first contract is binding and the second is not binding because it is deferred, as mentioned in al-Kifayah quoting al-Dhakhirah. So the custodian of the waqf may cancel the non-binding contracts if he fears the waqf will be lost due to a claim of ownership with the passage of time. Thus the commentator's objection is dismissed. Sadr al-Islam stated that the stratagem is to refer the matter to the judge so that he may validate it. Al-Sadr al-Shahid used to give fatwa permitting three years for farmland unless the interest is in prohibition, and for other than farmland, non-permissibility for more than one year unless the interest is in permission.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hila) for leasing waqf properties for long periods without violating the three-year rule. The method is to conclude multiple separate one-year contracts, e.g., thirty contracts each for one year, so that only the first contract is immediately binding. The later contracts are considered 'future' (mudaf) and thus not binding; the custodian (mutawalli) can cancel them if needed, e.g., if the lessee might claim ownership. This answers an objection raised by the commentator. The passage also notes alternative opinions: Sadr al-Islam suggests seeking a judge's approval, and al-Sadr al-Shahid differentiated between agricultural land (up to three years) and other properties (up to one year), considering maslaha.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result while complying with the letter of the law.
المتولي (al-mutawalli): Custodian or manager of waqf property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes the second contract non-binding in the Hanafi hila for waqf lease?
A) It exceeds three years
B) It is deferred (mudaf)
C) It lacks a fixed term
D) The lessee objects

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hila for long-term waqf lease uses multiple one-year contracts, with the first binding and the rest cancellable, allowing extended use without violating the three-year rule.

Specifying the Work in Ijara: Conditions for a Valid Contract
Definition-ruling on specifying the work in Ijara to ensure the benefit is known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the specifications required to make the benefit known in an Ijara contract for work and explain why each specification is necessary to avoid jahalah.

Core Idea
The benefit in Ijara must be known; detailing the work (fabric, color, style, load, distance) removes ambiguity and validates the contract.

In Hanafi fiqh, the subject of an Ijara contract must be clearly known. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains how specifying the work itself – from the type of fabric to the style of sewing – achieves this knowledge. Without such detail, the uncertainty (jahalah) renders the contract voidable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Recognize that this passage deals with Ijara where the subject is a specific work (e.g., dyeing, sewing, carrying). The benefit is the service itself.
2
Extract the specification requirements
From the examples, note each element: for garments – fabric type, dye color, sewing style; for carrying – load quantity, type of goods, distance.
The passage mentions four specifics for garments and three for carrying.
3
Understand the legal basis
The Hanafi principle requires that the benefit be ma'lum (known). These details collectively eliminate jahalah.
Jahalah is the prohibited uncertainty.
4
Identify the consequences of omission
If any of these specifications are missing, the contract is fasid (voidable) due to jahalah.
The passage does not mention minor omissions; all specification is required.
5
Apply to similar contracts
This rule applies to any Ijara for work, e.g., hiring a launderer, tailor, or transporter. Always specify the nature, quantity, and method.
The passage explicitly says 'of this type is the lease for work: like hiring a launderer'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبذكر العمل كصبغ ثوب وخياطته، وحمل قدر الإجارة على الأوقاف أن يعقد عقوداً متفرقة على الأوقاف كل عقد على سنة، فيكتب: استأجر فلان بن فلان كذا، بثلاثين عقداً، كل عقد على سنة، فيكون العقد الأول لازماً والثاني غير لازم؛ لأنه مضاف ذكره في الكفاية نقلاً عن الذخيرة. فلمتولي الوقف أن يفسح الإجارة في العقود الغير اللازمة إذا خاف بطلان الوقف لدعوى الملك بمرور الزمان، فعلى هذا اندفع اعتراض الشارح. وذكر صدر الإسلام له: إن الحيلة فيه أن يرفع إلى الحاكم حتى يجيزه، والصدر الشهيد له كان يفتي بالجواز في ثلاث سنين في الضياع، إلا إذا كانت المصلحة في المنع، وفي غير الضياع بعدم الجواز فيما زاد على سنة واحدة، إلا إذا كانت المصلحة في الجواز. قوله : وبذكر العمل ...الخ ؛ أي ويعلم النفع بذكر العمل، كصنع ثوب...الخ ؛ لأنه إذا بين الثوب بأنه من القطن أو الإبرسيم أو الصوف أو الكتان، ولون الصبغ بأنه أحمر أو أصفر ونحوهما ، وبين الخياطة بأنها فارسيّة أو روميّة، وبين القدر المحمول على الدابة، بأنه قنطاران أو نحوه، وجنس المحمول بأنه حنطة أو شعير، والمسافة صارت المنفعة معلومة بلا شبهة، فصح العقد، ومن هذا النوع الإجارة على العمل : كاستئجار القصار ونحوه.
And by mentioning the work, such as dyeing a garment and sewing it, and carrying a measure, the lease of waqf... [repetition of hila]. His saying: 'And by mentioning the work' etc., meaning the benefit is known by mentioning the work, such as making a garment, etc.; because if he specifies the garment as being of cotton, silk, wool, or linen, and the dye color as red or yellow, etc., and specifies the sewing as Persian or Roman, and specifies the load carried on the animal as two qintars or similar, and the type of load as wheat or barley, and the distance, then the benefit becomes known without doubt, so the contract is valid. And of this type is the lease for work: like hiring a launderer, etc.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling clarifying the requirement that in an Ijara contract, the work (amal) must be specified in detail so that the benefit (manfa'ah) becomes known (ma'lum). The text uses examples: dyeing and sewing a garment, carrying a load. For a garment, one must specify the fabric (cotton, silk, wool, linen), the dye color, and the type of sewing (Persian or Roman). For carrying, the load quantity, type, and distance must be stated. This level of detail removes ambiguity and validates the contract. The contract for work is a form of Ijara, e.g., hiring a launderer. The passage also includes an earlier hila discussion, but the main point is the specification requirement.

Key Terms
المنفعة (al-manfa'ah): The benefit or service that is the subject of an Ijara contract.
الجهالة (al-jahalah): Uncertainty or ambiguity in the contract subject that can render it invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
For an Ijara contract for work to be valid, the work must be specified in full detail (type, material, color, method, quantity, distance) to eliminate jahalah and make the benefit known.

Ijarah on Awqaf: The Valid Trick of Separate Contracts
Ruling on multiple ijarah contracts on endowments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on multiple ijarah contracts on awqaf and state the permissible trick (hila) used to avoid invalidity.

Core Idea
A sequence of one-year leases on an endowment is a permissible trick to preserve the waqf from claims of ownership.

In this lesson, we examine a practical issue: how can an endowment (waqf) be leased for long periods without risking claims of ownership? Classical Hanafi jurists devised a trick (hila) using multiple separate contracts. We will analyze the ruling, the conditions, and the scholarly differences within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Hila
State the overall ruling: the trick of making separate annual contracts is permissible (mubah) as a dispensation to preserve the endowment.
The hila involves writing multiple contracts, each for one year, so only the first is binding.
2
Understand the Problem
Long-term leases could lead to claims of ownership (e.g., through usucapion), potentially invalidating the waqf.
The waqf must remain perpetually; long possession might give the lessee a claim.
3
Examine the Contractual Structure
The first contract is binding (lazim); subsequent ones are non-binding (ghayr lazim) because they are future (mudaf). The trustee can cancel the non-binding ones if needed.
This structure prevents the lessee from acquiring a permanent right.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Differences
Sadr al-Islam suggests referring to a judge; Sadr al-Shahid allows up to 3 years on land and 1 year on other properties, contingent on maslahah.
These are valid variations within the school, both aiming to protect the waqf.
5
Evaluate the Epistemological Weight
This is a later fatwa (fatawa mutakhkhirin) and not from the Zahir al-Riwayah. It is accepted as mufta bihi for practical necessity.
The commentator's prayer 'May Allah forgive them' signals it is a concession, not a sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبذكر العمل كصبغ ثوب وخياطته، وحمل قدر الإجارة على الأوقاف أن يعقد عقوداً متفرقة على الأوقاف كـل عـقـد علـى سنة ، فيكتب: استأجر فلان بن فلان كذا، بثلاثين عقداً ، كل عقد على سنة، فيكون العقد الأول لازماً والثاني غير لازم ؛ لأنه مضاف ذكره في «الكفاية» نقلاً عن «الذخيرة». فلمتولي الوقف أن يفسح الإجارة في العقود الغير اللازمة إذا خاف بطلان الوقف لدعوى الملك بمرور الزمان، فعلى هذا اندفع اعتراض الشارح . وذكر صدر الإسلام له : إنّ الحيلة فيه أن يرفع إلى الحاكم حتى يجيزه ، والصدر الشهيد له كان يفتي بالجواز في ثلاث سنين في الضياع، إلا إذا كانت المصلحة في المنع ، وفي غير الضياع بعدم الجواز فيما زاد على سنة واحدة، إلا إذا كانت المصلحة في الجواز. ذكره الزيلعي، وغيره. ١ ] قوله : تجاوز الله عنهم ؛ هذا الدعاء مشعر بكون تجويزهم هذا معصية، والحال ما عرفت.
And by mentioning the work like dyeing a garment and sewing it, and the carrying of ijarah on endowments is that one makes separate contracts on the endowments, each contract for one year, so he writes: 'So-and-so has hired this and that with thirty contracts, each contract for one year.' Thus the first contract is binding and the second is not binding because it is future; this is mentioned in al-Kifayah quoting al-Dhakhirah. So the trustee of the endowment may release the ijarah in the non-binding contracts if he fears the invalidity of the endowment due to a claim of ownership with the passage of time. Thus the objector's objection is refuted. Sadr al-Islam said: The trick in this is to refer it to the judge so that he permits it. Sadr al-Shahid used to give fatwa of permissibility for three years on lands, unless there was a benefit in prohibiting it, and on non-lands, he ruled impermissible for more than one year, unless there was a benefit in permitting it. Al-Zayla'i and others mentioned it. His statement: 'May Allah forgive them' indicates that this permissibility is a sin, but the matter is as you know.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific issue regarding ijarah of awqaf (endowments). The commentator explains that to avoid problems from long-term leases that might lead to claims of ownership (due to usucapion), the trustee may make multiple consecutive one-year contracts. Only the first is binding; the rest are non-binding options. This is based on a trick (hila) that is permissible to prevent the endowment from being lost. Sadr al-Islam suggests taking the matter to a judge to permit it. Sadr al-Shahid differentiated: on agricultural land (al-diyāʿ), up to three years is allowed unless there is a reason to forbid; on other properties, only one year is allowed unless there is a benefit. The commentator notes that the author's prayer 'May Allah forgive them' suggests that this permissibility might be considered a sin, but the reality is that it is a permissible dispensation due to necessity.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf): An endowment; property dedicated to charity, whose benefit is given perpetually.
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal trick or stratagem used to achieve a lawful result while avoiding a prohibited or harmful consequence; here, to avoid long-term leases that might lead to claims of ownership.
متولي الوقف (mutawalli al-waqf): The trustee or administrator of the endowment who has authority to manage it.

Multiple separate one-year ijarah contracts on an endowment is a permissible Hanafi trick to preserve the waqf from ownership claims.

Specifying Benefit in Ijarah: By Work and By Indication
Commentary on how benefit is specified in ijarah contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two methods of specifying benefit in an ijarah contract and identify the conditions under which each method makes the contract valid.

Core Idea
Benefit must be known to avoid gharar; it can be specified by detailing the work or by indicating the destination.

In ijarah, the benefit (manfa'ah) must be clearly known for the contract to be valid. The Hanafi jurists detail two methods to achieve this: specifying the work with all necessary attributes, or indicating the subject matter and destination. This lesson unpacks the commentary's explanation of these methods and the subtle exceptions regarding details like dye quantity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods of specifying benefit
The passage mentions two methods: by mentioning the work (e.g., making a garment) and by indication (e.g., carrying to a place).
2
Break down the first method: detailing the work
For the first method, list the required specifications: type of fabric, colour of dye, style of sewing, weight and type of load, distance. If these are known, the benefit is known.
3
Examine the exception for dyeing
When the matn says 'like dyeing a garment', it indicates that the number of dips (i.e., quantity of dye) is not required if the dye is uniform. If it varies, then quantity must be stated.
This is from the reference to al-Kafi.
4
Analyze the second method: indication
The benefit is known by indicating the item and the destination, such as 'carry this food to that place.' This is similar to the first method.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The goal is to make the benefit known to avoid gharar (uncertainty). If any essential detail is omitted, the contract may be fasid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وبذكر العمل ...الخ ؛ أي ويعلم النفع بذكر العمل، كصنع ثوب...الخ ؛ لأنه إذا بين الثوب بأنه من القطن أو الإبرسيم أو الصوف أو الكتان، ولون الصبغ بأنه أحمر أو أصفر ونحوهما ، وبين الخياطة بأنها فارسيّة أو روميّة، وبين القدر المحمول على الدابة، بأنه قنطاران أو نحوه، وجنس المحمول بأنه حنطة أو شعير، والمسافة صارت المنفعة معلومة بلا شبهة، فصح العقد، ومن هذا النوع الإجارة على العمل : كاستئجار القصار ونحوه. قوله : كصيغ ثوب ؛ فيه إشعار بأنه لا يشترط بيان قدر الصبغ ، بأن يُبيِّن أنه يجعله في الصبغ مرة أو مرتين حتى يصير مشبعاً، وهذا إذا كان الصبغ مما لا يختلف، وإلا فيشترط قدره، كما أشير إليه في «الكافي»، وغيره. معلوم على دابة مسافة علمت ، وبالإشارة كنقل هذا إلى قمة. قوله : وبالإشارة... الخ ؛ يعني ويعلم النفعُ أيضاً بالإشارة كنقل هذا الطعام مثلاً إلى موضع فلاني ؛ لأنه إذا علم المنقول والمكان المنقول إليه صارت المنفعة معلومة، وهذا النوع قريب من النوع الأول.
His statement: 'And by mentioning the work... etc.' means the benefit is known by mentioning the work, such as making a garment... etc. Because when he specifies the garment as being of cotton, silk, wool, or linen, and the colour of the dye as red, yellow, or similar, and specifies the sewing as Persian or Roman, and specifies the load on the animal as two qintars or similar, and the type of load as wheat or barley, and the distance, the benefit becomes known without doubt, so the contract is valid. And of this type is ijarah for work, like hiring a fuller (washer) and the like. His statement: 'such as dyeing a garment' indicates that it is not required to specify the amount of dye, i.e., to state that he will dip it in the dye once or twice until it becomes saturated, if the dye is of a type that does not vary; otherwise, its quantity is required, as indicated in al-Kafi and others. Known on an animal over a known distance, and by indication such as carrying this to a peak. His statement: 'and by indication... etc.' means the benefit is also known by indication, such as carrying this food to a certain place, for when the item and the destination are known, the benefit becomes known, and this type is similar to the first type.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that states conditions for specifying the benefit (manfa'ah) in an ijarah contract. The author explains that the benefit is made known by specifying the work itself with all relevant details: the type of fabric, the colour of dye, the type of sewing, the weight and type of load, and the distance. When these are clear, the contract is valid. The commentator then addresses a specific point: when the matn mentions 'dyeing a garment', it indicates that it is not necessary to specify the number of dips if the dye does not vary; but if it does vary (e.g., different shades), then the quantity must be stated. This is referenced from al-Kafi. Finally, the commentator explains that benefit can also be known by indication, like saying 'carry this food to that place,' which makes the benefit similar to the first type.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A contract of leasing or hiring where one party pays for a known benefit from another party.
النفع (an-naf'): The benefit or utility that is the subject of the ijarah contract; it must be known to avoid gharar.
الصبغ (as-sibgh): Dye; the colouring agent used to dye fabric; the passage discusses its specification in ijarah for dyeing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Specifying the number of dips in the dye.
B) Specifying the colour of the dye.
C) Specifying the weight of the garment.
D) Specifying the type of sewing.

Key Takeaway
Benefit in ijarah must be known; it can be specified by detailing the work or by indicating the destination, with a nuance that uniform dye does not require quantity specification.

Specifying the Benefit through Indication in Ijarah
Explaining that the benefit can be known by indicating the item and destination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the benefit in an ijarah contract can be specified through indication (isharah) and identify the condition for its validity.

Core Idea
Knowing the object and destination by indication makes the benefit known in ijarah – precision without verbal description suffices.

In Hanafi fiqh, an ijarah contract requires the benefit (manfa'ah) to be known to avoid gharar (uncertainty). While detailed description is one method, this passage shows that simply indicating the item and destination also suffices. Understanding this ruling helps you grasp the flexibility of the Hanafi approach to specifying the subject matter in hire contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods of specifying the benefit
From the passage, note that the benefit can be known either by specifying the riding animal and the distance, or by clear indication (isharah) such as pointing to the item and its destination.
The first method is verbal description; the second is non-verbal but equally effective.
2
Understand the condition for valid indication
The passage states that when the thing being moved (manqul) and the place to which it is moved (makan) are known, the benefit becomes known. Thus, the indication must make both the subject matter and destination clear.
Ambiguity in either element would render the contract invalid (fasid).
3
Relate this to the broader principle
The author notes this type is 'close to the first type' – meaning it parallels the method of specifying by description. Both achieve the same legal effect: the benefit is ma'ruf (known).
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on substance over form in muamalat.

When the Wage Becomes Due in Ijarah: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute on wage ownership in ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when the wage becomes due in ijarah, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the wage in ijarah is not owned by the contract alone — it becomes due only upon delivery of the benefit.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya explains why the Hanafi school holds that the wage is not due by the contract itself, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i. The commentator clarifies the meaning of wujub and provides the practical consequence involving a slave relative.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
First, identify the core ruling: the wage is not obligatory by the contract. Note that 'obligatory' here means ownership (tamlik) is not established.
The commentator distinguishes between wujub asl (essence of obligation) and wujub ada' (obligation of payment).
2
Identify intra-Hanafi nuance
Notice the two narrations from Muhammad: in al-Jami' the wage is not owned regardless; in al-Ijarat it is owned if it is a debt. The majority follow al-Jami'.
This shows internal differences even within the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the Shafi'i position and proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the wage is owned by the contract itself, based on treating the non-existent benefit as existent by legal fiction. The contract of exchange immediately establishes ownership of both countervalues.
They cite the hadith prohibiting selling what is not possessed, but permitting salam, as a basis for making non-existent things legally existent.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: since the benefit is non-existent at contract formation, it cannot be the subject of immediate ownership. Balance (musawa) in exchange requires that the wage is due only when the benefit is delivered.
This is a rational (aqli) argument based on the nature of exchange contracts.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the wage is a slave who is the lessee's relative, under Hanafi the slave is not set free immediately because the wage is not yet owned; under Shafi'i, he is set free immediately, and the lessor can demand delivery of the slave.
This shows how a doctrinal difference leads to different legal outcomes in a concrete scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الأجر متى يستحق ولا تجب الأجرة بالعقد، خلافاً للشافعي، فإنَّ الأجرة عنده تجب بنفس العقد . قوله : ولا يجب الأجرة بالعقد : أي لا تملك سواء كانت عيناً أو ديناً. كذا ذكر محمد » في «الجامع»، وذكر في «الإجارات»: إن كانت عيناً لا تملك بالعقد، وإن كانت ديناً تملك به، ويكون بمنزلة الدين المؤجل، وعامة المشايخ على ما في «الجامع». كذا في «كمال الدراية». والمراد بالوجوب في قوله : لا يجب ; نفس الوجوب لا وجوب الأداء فإنّه عقد معاوضة، فيعتبر فيه المساواة، ولم توجد في جانب المعقود عليه، لا نفس الوجوب، ولا وجوب الأداء، فكذلك في جانب العوض. كذا في «الكفاية». قوله : خلافاً للشافعي، وفائدة الخلاف : أنه عندنا يثبت للمؤجر ولاية المطالبة بتسليمها في الحال، ولو كانت الأجرة عبداً وهو قريبه، لا يعتق عليه في الحال، وعنده له ولايةُ المطالبة في الحال، ويعتقُ عليه في الحال. كذا في «البناية». قوله : فإن الأجرة ... الخ ; يعني فإنَّ الأجرة عنده تملك بنفس العقد، ويجب تسليمها عند تسليم العين المستأجرة ; لأنها عقد معاوضة، فيثبت الملك في البدلين بنفس العقد. وهذا مبين على أصله أنَّ المنفعة المعدومة عنده جعلت موجودة حكماً ; لأن من شرط جواز العقد أن يكون المعقود عليه مقدوراً على تسليمه ; لأنه صلى الله عليه وسلم «نهى عن بيع ما ليس عند الإنسان»)، ورخص في السلم.
Chapter: When the wage is due. And the wage is not obligatory by the contract, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for the wage with him is obligatory by the contract itself. His saying: 'And the wage is not obligatory by the contract' means: it is not owned, whether it is a specific item or a debt. Thus Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami', and he mentioned in al-Ijarat: if it is a specific item, it is not owned by the contract, and if it is a debt, it is owned by it, and it is like a deferred debt. And the generality of the mashayikh are upon what is in al-Jami'. So in Kamal al-Dirayah. And the intended meaning of wujub in his saying 'not obligatory' is the essence of obligation, not the obligation of payment, for it is a contract of exchange, so equality is considered, and it is not found on the side of the subject matter, neither the essence of obligation nor the obligation of payment, so likewise on the side of the countervalue. So in al-Kifayah. His saying: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i', and the practical difference: that according to us, the lessor has the authority to demand its delivery immediately, and if the wage is a slave who is his relative, he is not set free immediately, while according to him, he has the authority to demand immediately, and he is set free immediately. So in al-Binayah. His saying: 'for the wage ... etc.' means: for the wage according to him is owned by the contract itself, and its delivery is obligatory upon delivery of the leased property; because it is a contract of exchange, so ownership of the two countervalues is established by the contract itself. And this is based on his principle that the non-existent benefit is treated as existent by legal fiction; because among the conditions for the validity of the contract is that the subject matter must be capable of delivery, for the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited selling what is not in one's possession, but permitted salam.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position, stated in the matn, is that the wage (ujrah) does not become due by the mere contract. The commentator clarifies that this means the wage is not owned by the contract, whether it is a specific item or a debt. There is a transmission from Muhammad in al-Jami' that both types are not owned, but in al-Ijarat he distinguishes: if it is a specific item, it is not owned; if it is a debt, it is owned but like a deferred debt. The majority of mashayikh follow al-Jami'. The term 'wujub' here refers to the obligation itself, not the obligation of payment, because this is a contract of exchange, so balance is required, and since the benefit is not yet delivered, the wage is not yet owed. The practical difference with Shafi'i is that according to us, the lessor does not have the right to demand delivery of the wage immediately; if the wage is a slave who is the lessee's relative, the slave is not set free immediately. According to Shafi'i, he has the right to demand immediately and the slave is set free immediately. The Shafi'i argument is based on treating non-existent benefit as existent by legal fiction, citing the hadith prohibiting selling what is not present but permitting salam.

Key Terms
الأجرة (al-ujrah): The wage or rental payment in an ijarah contract.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Obligation; here meaning the wage becomes due/owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the wage become due in an ijarah contract?
A) Immediately upon signing the contract
B) Only after the benefit is delivered
C) At the end of the lease period regardless of delivery
D) It is never due

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, the wage is not owned by the contract alone; it becomes due upon delivery of the benefit.

8 min
When the Wage Becomes Due in Ijara
Explains Hanafi ruling on timing of wage obligation in hire contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the wage in an ijara contract is not owned by the contract alone, and contrast this with the Shafi'i position, citing key proofs and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In hire, the wage follows the benefit: no benefit yet, no wage due—ownership is deferred.

When you rent a car, do you own the rent the moment you sign the contract, or only after you receive the car? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools famously disagree on this point. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, explaining why the wage is not obligated by the mere contract and how this leads to real-world differences in rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the matn says: the wage is not obligated by the contract. Note the immediate exception: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i'.
Pay attention to the word 'obligated' (wajib) meaning ownership, not payment.
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
Al-Shafi'i holds the wage is owned by the contract itself. His proof: benefit is considered legally existent (mawjud hukman) based on analogies like salam and inheritance for the fetus.
The Shafi'i principle is that what is non-existent can be made existent by the Lawgiver's treatment.
3
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: ijara is a commutative contract requiring equality. Since the benefit does not exist (it is an accident), it cannot be owned, so the wage cannot be owned either. The physical asset stands in for benefit only for validity and delivery, not for ownership.
The contract is 'added' (mudaf) to future moments of benefit occurrence.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: if the wage is a slave who is a relative of the lessor, in Hanafi he is not freed immediately; in Shafi'i he is freed immediately because ownership is instant.
This difference flows from the core dispute about the timing of ownership.

Source Text

باب الأجر متى يستحق
ولا تجب الأجرة بالعقد

والشارع جعل المعدوم حقيقة موجوداً حكماً ، كما جعل النطفة في الرحم كالحي في حق الأحكام من الوصية والإرث ، وكذا يجعلُ الموجود حقيقة كالمعدوم حكماً ، كما يجعل المرتد الملتحق بدار الحرب ميتاً في في حق الأحكام كالإرث وعتق أمهات أولاده، والدليل على أنه كالموجود جواز الاستئجار بالدين، ولو كان معدوماً لما جاز ؛ لأنه يصير ديناً بدين، وهو حرام شرعاً.

بل باعتبار أن العين التي هي سبب وجودِ المنفعة أقيمت مقام المنفعة في حق صحة الإيجاب والقبول، وفي حق وجوب التسليم ؛ إذ العين هي التي يمكن تسليمهما دون العرض، فإنعقد في حقها في الحال فوجب عليه تسليمها.
وصار العقد مضافاً غير منعقد للحال في حق المنفعة ؛ لأنَّ أقصى ما يتصور العقد على المنفعة أن يكون العقد مضافاً إلى وقت حدوثها ، فينعقد العقد في كل جزء من المنفعة على حسب وجودها شيئاً فشيئاً، وهو معنى قولنا : إنَّ عقد الإجارة في حكم عقود متفرّقة يتجدّد انعقادها على حسب حدوث المنافع.
وإنما قامت العين مقامَ المنفعة تصحيحاً للعقد في حق الانعقاد، والتسليم ضرورة عدم تصورهما في المنفعة، ولا ضرورةً في حق الملك في البدل إذما يثبت للضرورة يثبتُ بقدرها، فلا يظهر في حق ملك البدل، كما لا يظهرُ في حق ملك المنفعة، فكذا العقد مضافاً إلى وقت حدوثها، غير منعقد للحال في حقهما.
Chapter: When the wage becomes due. And the wage is not obligated by the contract, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for indeed the wage according to him is obligated by the contract itself. [1] His statement: 'The wage is not obligated by the contract' means: it is not owned, whether it is a specific object or a debt. Thus Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami', and he mentioned in al-Ijarat: If it is a specific object, it is not owned by the contract; if it is a debt, it is owned by it, and it is like a deferred debt. The majority of the mashayikh are upon what is in al-Jami'. Likewise in Kamal al-Diraya. And what is meant by 'obligation' in his statement 'not obligated' is the obligation itself, not the obligation of payment, for it is a commutative contract, so equality is required in it, and it is not found on the side of the object of the contract, neither the obligation itself nor the obligation of payment, so likewise on the side of the consideration. Likewise in al-Kifaya. (2) [1] His statement: 'Contrary to al-Shafi'i', and the benefit of the disagreement: that according to us, the lessor has the right to demand its delivery immediately, and if the wage was a slave who is his relative, he (the slave) is not set free immediately, while according to him (al-Shafi'i) he has the right to demand immediately and he (the slave) is set free immediately. Likewise in al-Binaya. [1] His statement: 'Indeed the wage...' etc.; meaning that according to him the wage is owned by the contract itself, and delivery is obligated upon delivery of the leased asset, because it is a commutative contract, so ownership of both substitutes is established by the contract itself. And this is based on his principle that a non-existent benefit is considered existent legally, because from the conditions of contract validity is that the object of the contract must be deliverable, because the Prophet ﷺ forbade selling what is not with you, and permitted salam. (References). And the Lawgiver made what is truly non-existent existent legally, just as He made the sperm in the womb like a living person in rulings such as bequest and inheritance, and likewise He makes what is truly existent like non-existent legally, as He makes the apostate who joins the abode of war dead in rulings such as inheritance and the setting free of ummahat awlad. And the evidence that it is like existent is the permissibility of hiring with a debt, because if it were non-existent it would not be permissible, because it would become a debt for a debt, which is forbidden by Shariah. And our proof: This is a commutative contract, so it requires equality between them, which is by the mutual exchange of the two substitutes in ownership and delivery. And one of the two substitutes, which is the benefit, does not become owned by the contract itself, because it is impossible to establish ownership (of a non-existent thing), so likewise the other substitute. And if he owned the wage, he would own it without a substitute, which is not the requirement of exchange, so deferral of ownership of it is necessary. And the validity of the contract is not because the non-existent is considered existent legally. How can that be said when the existent part of benefit cannot accept a contract; because it is an accident that does not remain for two moments of time, so delivery cannot be conceived for it, so it is not a locus for the contract. Rather, it is because the physical asset (al-'ayn) which is the cause of the existence of the benefit is made to stand in place of the benefit with respect to the validity of offer and acceptance, and with respect to the obligation of delivery. For the physical asset is what can be delivered, unlike the accident. So the contract is concluded with respect to it immediately, so delivery of it is obligated. And the contract becomes added (mudaf) but not concluded for the present with respect to the benefit. Because the utmost that can be conceived of a contract on benefit is that it be added to the time of its occurrence, so the contract is concluded for each part of the benefit according to its existence little by little. And this is the meaning of our statement: The contract of ijara is in the category of separate contracts, its conclusion being renewed according to the occurrence of the benefits. And the physical asset only stands in place of the benefit to validate the contract with respect to conclusion and delivery due to the necessity of the impossibility of these in the benefit, and there is no necessity with respect to ownership of the substitute. For what is established by necessity is established to the extent of the necessity, so it does not appear with respect to ownership of the substitute, just as it does not appear with respect to ownership of the benefit. So also the contract is added to the time of its occurrence, not concluded for the present with respect to both of them.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the timing of wage ownership in an ijara contract. The matn states: 'The wage is not obligated by the contract.' The commentator expands on this, explaining the Hanafi position and contrasting it with al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi position, based on Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' and al-Ijarat, distinguishes between a specific object ('ayn) and a debt (dayn). If the wage is a specific object, it is not owned by the contract; if a debt, it is owned like a deferred debt. The majority of Hanafi scholars follow al-Jami'. The rationale: ijara is a commutative contract requiring mutual exchange. Since the benefit (manfa'a) is not owned at the moment of contract (being a non-entity), the wage cannot be owned either, else it would be a benefit without countervalue. The Shafi'i view considers benefit as legally existent by default, analogizing to salam and other rulings. The Hanafi response argues that benefit is an accident (arad) that cannot be a direct subject of contract; rather, the physical asset (al-'ayn) stands in for the benefit for validity of offer/acceptance and delivery, but not for ownership. Thus the contract is 'added' (mudaf) to the future moments of benefit occurrence, and ownership of the wage follows accordingly.

Key Terms
أجرة (ujra): The wage or rent in an ijara contract; the countervalue for the benefit.
منفعة (manfa'a): The benefit or usufruct of the leased asset, which is an accident (arad) not enduring.
عقد معاوضة (aqd mu'awada): A commutative contract where each party gives and receives something of value.
عين ('ayn): A specific physical object (as opposed to a debt). In ijara, if the wage is a specific object, it is not owned by the contract.
دين (dayn): A debt, which is something owed in general terms (e.g., a measure of wheat). In ijara, if the wage is a debt, it is owned as a deferred debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if the wage in an ijara contract is a specific object (e.g., a horse), when is it owned by the lessor?
A) Immediately upon signing the contract.
B) Only after the leased asset is delivered and benefits begin.
C) It is never owned; only the right to use is owned.
D) It is owned at the end of the lease period.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the wage in an ijara contract is not owned by the mere contract; ownership is deferred to preserve the equality required in commutative exchanges.
